Time-Series Forecasting in Safety-Critical Environments:
An EU-AI-Act-Compliant Open-Source Package
Zeitreihenprognose in sicherheitskritischen Umgebungen:
Ein KI-VO-konformes Open-Source-Paket
Thomas
Bartz-Beielstein
Bartz & Bartz
GmbH, 51643 Gummersbach,
Deutschland
tbb@bartzundbartz.de
Eva
Bartz
Bartz & Bartz GmbH, 51643
Gummersbach,
Deutschland
eva.bartz@bartzundbartz.de
?abstractname?
With
spotforecast2-safe
we present an
integrated
Compliance-by-Design
approach to Python-based point
forecasting of time series in safety-critical environments. A review of
the relevant open-source tooling shows that existing compliance solutions
operate consistently outside of the library to be used — e.g. as
scanners, templates, or runtime layers.
spotforecast2-safe
takes the inverse approach and anchors the requirements of Regulation
(EU) 2024/1689 (the EU AI Act, in German:
KI-VO
), of IEC 61508,
of the ISA/IEC 62443 standards series, and of the Cyber Resilience Act
within
the library: in application-programming-interface
contracts, persistence formats, and continuous-integration gates. The
approach is operationalised by four non-negotiable code-development rules
(zero dead code, deterministic processing, fail-safe handling, minimal
dependencies) together with the corresponding process rules (model card,
executable docstrings, CI workflows, Common-Platform-Enumeration (CPE)
identifier, REUSE-conformant licensing, release pipeline). Interactive
visualisation, hyperparameter tuning and automated machine learning (AutoML), as well as
deep-learning and large-language-model backends are deliberately
excluded, because each of these components either enlarges the attack
surface, introduces non-determinism, or impairs reproducibility. A
bidirectional traceability matrix maps every regulatory provision onto
the corresponding mechanism in the code; an end-to-end example of
European-market electricity generation, transmission, and consumption
forecasting demonstrates the application. The package is open-source and
available under Affero General Public License (AGPL) 3.0-or-later.
Keywords:
Compliance-by-Design, Artificial Intelligence,
Critical Infrastructure, Open Source, Time-Series Forecasting, Cyber
Resilience Act, Legal-Requirements-Engineering, safety-critical machine
learning, EU AI Act, deterministic computing, skforecast, spotforecast2,
Legal Aspects of Computing, Software Engineering, Software Safety,
Software Security
Note:
This is the English version of the paper.
A German version (
Zeitreihenprognose in sicherheitskritischen
Umgebungen
) follows further down in the same PDF. Both versions
share a single bibliography, printed once at the end of the document.
1
Introduction
The practice of software development in the field of artificial
intelligence (AI
1
1
1
In what follows we use AI as an umbrella term
covering machine learning, learning with deep neural networks, and
language models.
) and in the field of critical
infrastructure
2
2
2
The definition of KRITIS follows §2 of the
German law on the Federal Office for Information Security and on the
information security of facilities (BSI-Gesetz - BSIG) and §2 of the
framework law for the resilience of critical installations
(Kritis-Dachgesetz - KRITISDachG), together with the IT Security Act
2.0 and the act transposing the NIS-2 directive. KRITIS therefore
refers to a service for the supply of the general public in sectors
whose failure or impairment would lead to significant supply shortages
or to a threat to public safety
[
10
]
.
has, until
now, evolved largely in separate software ecosystems. AI-based software
development for time-series forecasting favours feature-rich libraries
that bundle visualisation, hyperparameter search, probabilistic
deep-learning backends, and interactive dashboards into a single
installable package. Such broad libraries are excellently suited to
exploratory work and innovative research. However, this richness turns
into a liability the moment the resulting forecasting model is deployed
in an environment that must withstand audits for safety-critical
systems. Visualisation tools, for instance, require the inclusion of a
larger number of additional graphics libraries. The same holds for the
hyperparameter-tuning layer and for deep-learning models, which
additionally exhibit very short release cycles, so that the long-term
reproducibility that regulators demand is not easily ensured.
A concrete review of the 2026 Python forecasting landscape confirms this
tension. The three dominant Python libraries,
sktime
[
63
]
,
Darts
[
33
]
, and
skforecast
[
2
]
, ship extensive functionality that goes far beyond
forecasting per se. The classical libraries (
statsmodels
,
pmdarima
) are leaner but offer neither dedicated fail-safe
semantics for invalid input nor a fixed CPE identifier for vulnerability
tracking.
When such software libraries are deployed in safety-critical
environments, the regulatory requirements that arise — among others
— from Regulation (EU) 2024/1689 on artificial intelligence (referred
to below as the “EU AI Act” or, in German,
KI-VO
) must be
taken into account. The EU AI Act follows a
risk-based
approach:
the regulatory intervention is tailored to the concrete risk level, not
to the technology as such
[
76
]
.
Figure
1
arranges the four resulting tiers
(unacceptable, high, limited, and low or minimal risk) in their
canonical depiction as a
pyramid of risk
.
Abbildung 1:
The four risk tiers of the EU AI Act
in their canonical pyramid depiction
[
24
]
. The depth of the
regulatory intervention rises with the risk level: outright prohibition
at the top, no obligations at the base.
spotforecast2-safe
is
designed for the second-highest tier (
high risk
).
In detail, the EU AI Act defines the four risk tiers — in heavily
simplified form — as follows:
•
Unacceptable risk.
The EU AI Act prohibits manipulative AI
systems using subliminal techniques, the targeted exploitation of
vulnerable groups, social scoring by public authorities, as well as
real-time remote biometric identification in publicly accessible
spaces for law-enforcement purposes (subject to narrow exceptions).
•
High risk.
This category covers AI systems acting as a safety
component of, or being themselves, a product regulated by existing EU
product-safety legislation (toys, aviation, motor vehicles, medical
devices, lifts), as well as systems deployed in the use cases listed
in Annex III of the EU AI Act. Providers must conduct an ex-ante
conformity assessment, register the system in an EU database, and
implement seven substantive requirements: EU-1. human oversight,
EU-2. technical robustness and safety, EU-3. privacy and data
governance, EU-4. transparency, EU-5. diversity, non-discrimination
and fairness, EU-6. social and environmental well-being, and EU-7. accountability. These seven requirements derive from the
Ethics Guidelines for Trustworthy AI
of the High-Level Expert
Group on Artificial Intelligence (AI-HLEG)
[
34
]
and are
operationalised by the
Assessment List for Trustworthy AI
(ALTAI)
[
35
,
76
]
.
•
Limited risk.
For chatbots, emotion-recognition systems,
biometric categorisation, and for systems that generate or manipulate
image, audio, or video content (so-called
deepfakes
),
transparency obligations apply: users must be able to recognise that
they are interacting with an AI system.
•
Low or minimal risk.
No additional obligations; the EU AI Act
encourages voluntary codes of conduct under which providers of
non-high-risk systems may adopt the requirements imposed on high-risk
systems.
Tabelle 1:
Semantic mapping of EU AI Act articles to the seven high-risk
requirements. EU-5 (diversity and non-discrimination) and EU-6 (social
and environmental well-being) are not addressed by an own EU-AI-Act row
in Table
LABEL:tbl-compliance-en
, because they do not trigger an
immediate implementation obligation for the software package presented
here.
EU AI Act article
High-risk requirement
Rationale
Art. 9 risk management
EU-7
Accountability — risk-management
system as a procedural evidence framework
Art. 10 data governance
EU-3
Privacy and data governance
Art. 11 technical documentation
EU-7
Accountability through
documentation
Art. 12 record-keeping obligations
EU-7
Accountability through
logs
Art. 13 transparency
EU-4
Transparency
Art. 14 human oversight
EU-1
Human oversight
Art. 15 accuracy, robustness, cybersecurity
EU-2
Technical
robustness and safety
Art. 17 quality-management system (QMS)
EU-7
Accountability
(umbrella)
Art. 18–21 record-keeping obligations of providers
EU-7
Accountability
Art. 72 post-market monitoring
EU-7
Accountability
Art. 73 reporting
EU-7
Accountability
The open-source software library
spotforecast2-safe
presented
in this report is designed as a component for high-risk forecasting
applications, e.g. for load forecasting in grid operations.
Table
LABEL:tbl-kivo-eu-mapping-en
shows the semantic mapping of the EU
AI Act articles to the seven high-risk requirements (EU-1 to EU-7). The
EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges providers and deployers of high-risk
AI systems to demonstrate compliance with all seven high-risk
requirements. When AI-research-grade software packages are used, a
deployer
3
3
3
Depending on the context, a deployer may additionally
take on the role of a provider.
inherits none of these demonstrations
from the software library itself. Such demonstrations may have to be
re-established at considerable expense.
By contrast,
spotforecast2-safe
shifts this evidence-bearing
inside the software library, so that the deployer inherits it. To this
end, the design principles described in
Chapter
4
are introduced. These principles
require a specific engineering decision and an accompanying test. The
discipline of translating regulatory provisions into implementable,
verifiable software requirements while maintaining a bidirectional
traceability between every provision and the mechanism that fulfills it
is known as
Legal-Requirements-Engineering
(LRE)
[
70
,
9
]
.
[
61
]
provides a systematic survey of the field.
LRE ensures that all relevant statutes, directives, recitals,
explanatory background, and standards are integrated into the software
development process to deliver sustained legal compliance
[
32
]
. LRE means that legal requirements should be considered
from the very design stage of an AI system. This avoids tedious and
potentially expensive adjustments at later stages of development
(
compliance-by-design
). Chapter
6.3
shows how the
spotforecast2-safe
software applies the LRE discipline.
While compliance-monitoring libraries such as
Deepchecks
[
13
]
and
Evidently AI
4
4
4
https://github.com/evidentlyai/evidently
partially cover the evaluation side of the robustness requirement under
Art. 15 EU AI Act,
5
5
5
The EU AI Act is in the following Regulation
(EU) 2024/1689 on artificial intelligence, also referred to as the
EU AI Act.
they sit on top of an already finished forecasting
software and alter neither the provenance trail, nor determinism, nor
the dependency footprint of the software itself.
Chapter
B.1
and Chapter
B.2
(in
the appendix) show how the
spotforecast2-safe
software
presented here can be combined with the compliance-monitoring features
of
Deepchecks
and
Evidently AI
.
A software library that rests on a software stack with dozens of
unpinned dependencies is not unconditionally uncertifiable; the cost of
constructing the required evidence trail, however, grows with every
additional dependency. The pre-existing standards on functional
safety
6
6
6
IEC 61508-3 for electrical and electronic systems
[
46
]
and the road-vehicle-specific adaptation
ISO 26262-6
[
50
]
.
make the same connection visible from
the opposite direction: software whose behaviour depends on
uncontrolled external factors cannot be classified above Safety
Integrity Level (SIL) 1
[
47
]
or Automotive Safety
Integrity Level (ASIL) A
[
49
]
without a
disproportionately large verification effort.
Cyber Resilience Act.
The requirements EU-1 to EU-7 defined by
the EU AI Act are, however, not the only regulatory metrics that a
forecasting library has to satisfy in order to be deployable in
safety-critical environments. In industrial deployment contexts, a
forecasting library runs as a software component on level 2 or 3 of the
Purdue reference model
7
7
7
A reference model for automation and
industrial networks; Table
5
maps the levels
to the relevant ISA/IEC 62443 parts.
(ISA-95 / IEC 62264), where the
dominant cybersecurity framework is the ISA/IEC 62443 standards series
[
39
]
.
8
8
8
The standards series distributes
responsibilities among asset owners (62443-2-1
[
45
]
), service providers (62443-2-4
[
44
]
), system integrators (62443-3-2
[
43
]
, 62443-3-3
[
40
]
), and component
manufacturers (62443-4-1
[
41
]
, 62443-4-2
[
42
]
); the latter two parts are directly applicable
to a software vendor.
Within the European Union (EU), the 62443
series — and EN IEC 62443-4-1 in particular — has, by Commission
Implementing Decision C(2025) 618 of 3 February 2025
[
20
]
,
been formally designated as a reference standard within the framework
of the standardisation request M/606 for harmonisation under the
now-in-force Cyber Resilience Act (
CRA
)
[
25
]
; it
furthermore shapes the risk-management duties under Directive (EU)
2022/2555 (
NIS-2 Directive
)
[
21
]
as well as the
Critical Entities Resilience Directive (CER Directive)
[
22
]
.
[
28
]
characterises 62443-2-1 as the
“equivalent of ISO/IEC 27001 for cyber-physical systems (CPS)”. The
part of the standards series directly binding for
spotforecast2-safe
, however, is 62443-4-1 (see also
Chapter
5.7.2
).
Civil-law product liability.
Beyond the public-law conformity
and cybersecurity frameworks, the new Product Liability Directive (PLD;
Directive (EU) 2024/2853)
[
23
]
extends strict product
liability to software and AI systems: these are, for the first time,
explicitly captured as a “product” within the meaning of liability
law, including integrated digital services and security-relevant
post-market updates. The directive replaces the 1985 Product Liability
Directive 85/374/EEC and applies to products placed on the market or
put into service after 9 December 2026; by that date the Member States
must also have completed national transposition. In Germany, the
directive is being implemented by the draft act on the modernisation of
product-liability law
[
17
]
, which at the time of this report
is undergoing parliamentary procedure (Bundestag printed paper
21/4297, first reading on 4 March 2026). Open-source software developed
or made available outside of a commercial activity remains exempt from
product liability. For a library released under AGPL-3.0-or-later such
as
spotforecast2-safe
, liability arises only where it is made
available on the Union market in the course of a commercial activity
— but there it carries the same duties of care and documentation that
the CRA and the EU AI Act impose.
Open-source carve-outs.
There is an almost unsurveyable thicket
of regulations that providers and deployers of AI models or systems may
have to take into account. The most prominent are Art. 50 and 51 of the
EU AI Act, the EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR), the Data
Act, and the violation of copyright, personality, or labour rights.
This contribution deliberately confines itself, as a first foray of
free and open-source software (FOSS) into KRITIS, to the regulations
mentioned at the outset.
•
EU AI Act: open-source special rules.
On top of the FOSS
exemption of the PLD, the EU AI Act and the CRA each contain their
own, mutually independent special rules for FOSS that are relevant
for
spotforecast2-safe
as a library released under
AGPL-3.0-or-later. Art. 2(12) of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
excludes AI systems released under a free or open-source licence in
principle from the regulation’s scope, but lifts that exception again
for high-risk AI systems within the meaning of Annex III, for
prohibited practices under Art. 5 of the EU AI Act, and for
transparency-bound systems under Art. 50 of the EU AI Act. In the
typical KRITIS use case of a load forecast within the meaning of
Annex III No. 2 (safety component of critical infrastructure), the
exception therefore does not apply; the package must satisfy the
substantive requirements discussed in Chapter
6.1.2
through Chapter
6.1.8
. Independently thereof,
Art. 25(4), last subparagraph of the EU AI Act explicitly does not
extend the obligation of a written value-chain agreement between
component supplier and provider of a high-risk system to tools,
services, processes, or components made available under a free or
open-source licence, provided they do not concern general-purpose AI
(GPAI) models; the library therefore owes its downstream provider no
contractually regulated cooperation, but supplies the
cooperation-enabling preconditions through open provenance,
documented interfaces, and reproducible releases (see
Chapter
4
). The GPAI-specific FOSS exemptions in
Art. 53(2) and Art. 54(6) of the EU AI Act do not apply to a
classical regression library such as
spotforecast2-safe
,
because the package does not place any GPAI model in the sense of
Art. 3 No. 63 of the EU AI Act on the market.
•
CRA: open-source special rules.
The CRA
[
25
]
complements this scheme via Art. 24 CRA, which provides for reduced
obligations for “open-source software stewards” — typically
not-for-profit or community-economic organisations that maintain
FOSS projects on a long-term basis — relative to the manufacturer
duties of Art. 13 ff. CRA (a documented cybersecurity policy,
coordinated vulnerability handling, cooperation with market
surveillance) and excludes the imposition of administrative fines
except for reporting duties. Recital 18 CRA further clarifies that
the mere financial support of a FOSS project does not preclude its
classification as non-monetised.
•
NIS-2: open-source special rules.
NIS-2
[
21
]
provides
no explicit FOSS exemption; its supply-chain duty under
Art. 21(2)(d) NIS-2 binds the regulated KRITIS operator and not the
open-source maintainer, but propagates de facto into the FOSS
ecosystem through contractual requirements. The relevant legal
literature systematically analyses this entanglement
[
15
,
78
,
60
]
.
Trustworthiness.
A parallel research line addresses
how
the trustworthiness of a high-risk AI system is to be assessed.
[
76
]
propose a trustworthiness-assurance framework that
decomposes the EU AI Act requirements through a six-step iterative
process into verifiable properties and connects AI-system engineering
with the assurance argumentation of functional safety. The present
report is compatible with this framework: every process rule in
Chapter
4
is an argumentation step, and the
Mechanism
column of Table
LABEL:tbl-compliance-en
provides the
substantiation.
Artificial intelligence and fundamental rights.
The
legal-scholarship background is set out by
[
75
]
, who examines
the interplay between the EU AI Act and the Charter of Fundamental
Rights of the European Union, anti-discrimination law, and data
protection law.
[
75
]
is the authoritative reference when a
deployer has to translate the technical evidence documented here into
a Fundamental Rights Impact Assessment (FRIA) under Art. 27 of the
EU AI Act.
Table
LABEL:tbl-normen-einleitung-en
summarises the legal acts,
standards, and reference models cited in the preceding paragraphs by
regulatory level.

2022/2557; CPS = cyber-physical system; ISMS = information-security

.

).

The
spotforecast2-safe
package attempts to bridge these gaps
between software development in AI research and in safety-critical
applications. The starting point for the development of
spotforecast2-safe
is
skforecast
[
2
]
.
About one third of the safety-critical lines of code are a direct port
from the
skforecast
source code. Additional code implements
the four code-development rules described in
Chapter
4.1
(“no dead code”, “deterministic
transformations”, “fail-safe handling”, and “minimal scope”), as
well as the four process rules described in
Chapter
4.2
. Because of these rules, the feature
scope of the software is reduced. To compensate for this deliberate
reduction,
spotforecast2-safe
is published as a subset of the
broader open-source sister package
spotforecast2
[
5
]
. The
spotforecast2
package adds further
features: interactive visualisation based on
plotly
and
matplotlib
, hyperparameter tuning via
optuna
and
spotoptim
, and model-explanation tooling based on
shap
.
The remainder of this report describes the subset implemented by
spotforecast2-safe
. The report pursues three objectives.
First, it situates the package within the landscape of Python
time-series tools and explains which features have been deliberately
omitted from the safety-critical subset and why
(Chapter
2
). Second, it documents the architecture
(Chapter
3.1
), the formal preprocessing and
forecasting algorithms (Chapter
3.2
through
Chapter
3.4
), and the safety-relevant design decisions
shipped in the most recent versions
(Chapter
4
through Chapter
5
).
Third, it maps the design decisions onto individual provisions of the
EU AI Act and of the functional-safety standards
(Chapter
6
) and closes with a brief
example of electric load forecasting
(Chapter
7
). The report is itself part of the
audit narrative: every code block is executed at the time of document
generation, and the entire document including the bibliography is under
version control.
2
Software packages for time-series forecasting
The Python time-series-forecasting ecosystem has grown strongly over
the past decade and has also adopted implementations from the
statistical programming language R
[
74
]
. Foundational is the
statsmodels
package, which provides ARIMA, SARIMAX, and
exponential-smoothing models in line with the classical exposition by
[
8
]
.
scikit-learn
[
72
]
deliberately
abstains from time-series-specific functionality and delegates such
features to downstream libraries. The most relevant of these libraries
are introduced briefly below.
skforecast
[
2
]
provides a
Forecaster
abstraction that wraps any scikit-learn-compatible regressor into a
recursive, direct, or multi-series forecaster. It is the closest
conceptual relative of the package documented here. From it we adopt
the architecture of recursive forecasting, the rolling-origin splitter,
and substantial parts of the modules
_binner
,
_differentiator
, and the residuals infrastructure. We do not
adopt the visualisation subsystem (which depends on
matplotlib
and
plotly
) or the hyperparameter tuning via
bayesian_search_forecaster
(which relies on
optuna
and produces non-deterministic optimisation traces). These tools are
contained in the
spotforecast2
package.
The unified
Forecaster
/
BaseEstimator
API of
sktime
[
63
,
64
]
, which provides a wide range
of models and validation schemes, is the most extensive of the
libraries considered here. However, the API is not fully compatible
with that of
scikit-learn
, so that integrating regressors from
that ecosystem is not straightforward. Moreover, many models in
sktime
rely on
statsmodels
implementations that are
not optimised for the requirements of functional safety (e.g. no
fail-safe handling of invalid inputs). Visualisation tools and
probabilistic backends are likewise absent.
The Nixtla ecosystem, which targets improvements in runtime throughput,
implements three complementary libraries:
statsforecast
[
30
]
provides vectorised implementations of classical
models, trading model flexibility for aggressive runtime optimisation
via Numba
[
62
]
.
mlforecast
extends the same API to
gradient-boosting methods.
neuralforecast
offers
deep-learning architectures through a unified interface. The
deep-learning backend immediately violates the rule of minimal scope.
Darts
[
33
]
introduces a unified API for various
time-series models. Its installation footprint is dominated by optional
dependencies whose behaviour influences the availability of model
classes at import time. The same applies to
tsai
,
Merlion
, and
Kats
[
69
,
7
,
58
]
.
None of the libraries listed is deficient from an engineering
standpoint. On the contrary, they constitute outstanding research-grade
software. However, software optimised for rapid exploration cannot
automatically meet a SIL-3 software verification budget without
substantial deployer-specific rework.
spotforecast2-safe
shifts that rework into the package itself, so that the deployer
inherits the demonstrations rather than having to reproduce them.
3
The
spotforecast2-safe
package
3.1
Architecture
The
spotforecast2-safe
package is organised into three layers.
The lowest layer (
forecaster/
,
preprocessing/
,
model_selection/
,
data/
) contains the estimator
wrappers, transformers, and splitters that derive most directly from
skforecast
. A middle layer (
manager/
) bundles an
estimator together with an
ExogBuilder
, a configurator, a
logger, and a persistence layer into a deployable unit. The top layer
(
processing/
and
tasks/
) provides end-to-end
pipelines that compose the two lower layers into console scripts.
src/spotforecast2_safe/
forecaster/
recursive/
base.py, utils.py, metrics.py
preprocessing/
repeating_basis_function.py
_binner.py, _differentiator.py
curate_data.py, imputation.py
linearly_interpolate_ts.py
outlier.py, split.py
model_selection/
split_ts_cv.py, split_one_step.py
validation.py
manager/
models/, configurator/, exo/
persistence.py, trainer.py, predictor.py
processing/, tasks/
downloader/, weather/
utils/, exceptions.py
The public interface is fixed by
__all__
at the top
package level and currently comprises eleven symbols:
Period
,
RepeatingBasisFunction
,
ExogBuilder
,
LinearlyInterpolateTS
, three
ForecasterRecursive*
model classes,
ConfigEntsoe
, the
alias
Config
, and the two helper items
__version__
and
hello
. Every API-breaking change
to these symbols requires a Conventional-Commits subject
feat!:
and triggers a major version bump via Semantic-Release. Private modules
(anything with a leading underscore or in a submodule that is not
re-exported at the top level) are explicitly unstable and excluded from
this guarantee.
Adding a new estimator wrapper therefore happens in the directory
manager/models/
: the wrapper inherits from
ForecasterRecursiveModel
, which itself orchestrates
fit
,
predict
,
backtest
, and
save
/
load
. The wrapper is exclusively responsible for
declaring the backend regressor and any backend-specific serialisation
hooks. The exogenous-feature layer, the rolling-origin validation, and
the audit logging are inherited.
The package shares its core with the (upstream) sister package
spotforecast2
[
5
]
. Conversely, the safe
subset adds four sub-packages that are not present in
spotforecast2
(
datasets/
,
downloader/
,
processing/
, and
weather/
), because they codify the
ENTSO-E and weather-data retrieval on which the audit narrative of
Chapter
5
builds. The dependency direction is
one-way.
spotforecast2-safe
does not import from
spotforecast2
; it is independently installable and testable.
spotforecast2
re-exports selected public symbols of
spotforecast2-safe
(in particular
ConfigEntsoe
), so
that a deployer who chooses the upstream package finds a consistent
configuration surface. This split makes it possible to release the
safe subset on its own cadence and to certify it independently, while
the upstream package remains a strict superset for the broader,
non-regulated use case.
3.2
Preprocessing
The
preprocessing
sub-package contains four kinds of
transformer: cyclical encoders that map periodic calendar features
(hour of day, day of week, day of year) into a smooth representation;
interpolators that reconstruct short gaps under an explicit contract;
a quantile binner that discretises a continuous feature into
equal-frequency bins; and a differentiator that applies a
finite-difference operator. All four transformers inherit from
sklearn.base.TransformerMixin
and are therefore composable
within an
sklearn
Pipeline
.
3.3
Forecasting
The forecasting layer implements the recursive multi-step strategy: a
single scalar one-step-ahead model is trained and then iterated to
produce horizons of arbitrary length
[
37
]
. For
y
t
y_{t}
,
lags
ℒ
=
{
ℓ
1
,
…
,
ℓ
p
}
\mathcal{L}=\{\ell_{1},\ldots,\ell_{p}\}
, window features
w
t
w_{t}
(e.g. moving averages), and an exogenous vector
x
t
x_{t}
, the
forecaster learns
y
^
t
=
f
​
(
y
t
−
ℓ
1
,
…
,
y
t
−
ℓ
p
,
w
t
,
x
t
)
,
\hat{y}_{t}=f\!\left(y_{t-\ell_{1}},\ldots,y_{t-\ell_{p}},\,w_{t},\,x_{t}\right),
and the
h
h
-step forecast is obtained recursively:
y
^
t
+
h
=
f
​
(
y
~
t
+
h
−
ℓ
1
,
…
,
y
~
t
+
h
−
ℓ
p
,
w
t
+
h
,
x
t
+
h
)
,
\hat{y}_{t+h}=f\!\left(\tilde{y}_{t+h-\ell_{1}},\ldots,\tilde{y}_{t+h-\ell_{p}},\,w_{t+h},\,x_{t+h}\right),
where
y
~
s
=
y
s
\tilde{y}_{s}=y_{s}
for
s
≤
t
s\leq t
and
y
~
s
=
y
^
s
\tilde{y}_{s}=\hat{y}_{s}
otherwise.
The advantage of recursive strategies in a safety-critical context is
that the training and evaluation paths are coupled by construction:
the same feature-matrix builder produces both the training features
and the features used at prediction time, so that feature-skew errors
become a build-time rather than a run-time error class. In addition, a
separate model can be trained for each individual prediction quantity
to achieve better accuracy, at the cost of an
h
h
-fold number of
models.
Prediction intervals are computed by bootstrap-resampling the
in-sample residuals. For a desired coverage
1
−
α
1-\alpha
, the
residuals
ε
1
,
…
,
ε
N
\varepsilon_{1},\ldots,\varepsilon_{N}
are drawn with
replacement and added to the recursive point forecast; this yields an
empirical interval between the
α
/
2
\alpha/2
and
1
−
α
/
2
1-\alpha/2
quantiles. This is the same procedure as in
skforecast
; given
a seed, it is deterministic and does not require a probabilistic
regressor.
The backend regressors shipped with the package are LightGBM
[
59
]
and XGBoost
[
12
]
, implemented as
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM
and
ForecasterRecursiveXGB
respectively. Both are strong baselines on M5-style electricity and
trade datasets
[
67
]
. Any scikit-learn-compatible estimator
can be deployed by direct instantiation of
ForecasterRecursiveModel
. The “M-competitions”
[
66
,
67
]
have established themselves as the de-facto
benchmark for point and interval forecasting. Models such as LightGBM
[
59
]
, XGBoost
[
12
]
, and their ensembles have
proven powerful baselines across a variety of application domains.
The decision to restrict the package’s supported backends to LightGBM,
XGBoost, and scikit-learn regressors (and to ship
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM
and
ForecasterRecursiveXGB
as the two default models) follows
directly from this empirical evidence.
3.4
Testing
A naive application of
sklearn.model_selection.KFold
to a
time-indexed series leaks the future into the training set.
Time-series-aware splitters remedy this by forcing the training
segment to precede the test segment in time. The package provides two
such splitters as well as a backtesting driver.
TimeSeriesFold
realises the growing-window protocol. Let
T
0
T_{0}
be
initial_train_size
,
s
s
be
steps
, and
h
h
the forecast horizon. The splitter yields train/test index pairs
of the form
(
[
0
,
T
0
+
k
⋅
s
)
,
[
T
0
+
k
⋅
s
,
T
0
+
k
⋅
s
+
h
)
)
([0,\,T_{0}+k\cdot s),\,[T_{0}+k\cdot s,\,T_{0}+k\cdot s+h))
for
k
=
0
,
1
,
2
,
…
k=0,1,2,\ldots
. A
refit
flag determines whether
the forecaster is re-trained at each fold or fitted only once at the
beginning. For scenarios in which even the refit cost is prohibitive,
the parameter
fold_stride
permits non-overlapping folds that
nevertheless cover a large test region; refit work is thus amortised
across multiple test regions. Figure
2
visualises a
typical configuration.
import
numpy
as
np
import
matplotlib.pyplot
as
plt
n_total
=
80
+
24
*
6
initial_train
=
80
steps
=
24
n_folds
=
6
fig, ax
=
plt.subplots(figsize
=
(
5.5
,
2.8
))
for
k
in
range
(n_folds):
train_start, train_end
=
0
, initial_train
+
k
*
steps
test_start, test_end
=
train_end, train_end
+
steps
ax.barh(k, train_end
-
train_start, left
=
train_start, color
=
"#4C72B0"
, height
=
0.7
)
ax.barh(k, test_end
-
test_start, left
=
test_start, color
=
"#DD8452"
, height
=
0.7
)
ax.set_yticks(
range
(n_folds))
ax.set_yticklabels([
f"Fold
{
k
+
1
}
"
for
k
in
range
(n_folds)])
ax.set_xlabel(
"Observation index"
)
ax.invert_yaxis()
ax.set_xlim(
0
, n_total)
fig.tight_layout()
plt.show()
Abbildung 2:
Rolling-origin backtesting folds of
TimeSeriesFold
(initial training 80, step size 24, six
folds). Training segments are shown in blue, held-out test segments
in orange. The training set grows with every fold while the held-out
fold advances by
steps
observations.
OneStepAheadFold
is a slim specialisation with
steps=1
, typically used for evaluating the conditional-mean
regressor itself (rather than the horizon-
h
h
point forecast).
backtesting_forecaster
composes one of the two splitters
with a
ForecasterRecursive*
together with a list of metrics
(
mean_absolute_error
,
mean_squared_error
,
mean_absolute_scaled_error
[
38
]
) and returns a
pandas DataFrame of fold-wise metric values together with the
concatenated forecast vector. Parallelised refit execution is offered
via
joblib
and obliges the caller to fix
n_jobs
;
this is the only point in the package where parallelism is exposed,
and the fixing is mandatory because OpenMP scheduling is a known
source of cross-platform non-determinism.
[
6
]
provides the theoretical justification: under
standard stationarity assumptions, the forward-chaining
cross-validation estimator of the mean-squared one-step-ahead
prediction error is consistent, whereas the ordinary
k
k
-fold
cross-validation estimator is not. The package therefore exposes
exclusively the two time-aware splitters; from the manager layer there
is no escape route to the
sklearn
originals.
4
Design principles
The design principles fall into two parallel categories. The
code-development rules
restrict which constituents the source
code may contain; they are enforced at commit time by a test or by a
linter
9
9
9
A linter is a static-analysis tool that checks the
source code, without executing it, for style violations, suspicious
patterns, and breaches of programming conventions. In the present
package,
ruff
,
black
,
isort
, and
mypy
are invoked via pre-commit hooks.
. The
process rules
restrict how the package is developed, shipped,
and operated, and are met by
MODEL_CARD.md
, CI workflows,
and the release pipeline rather than by source-code tests. Both
categories are required: the code-development rules alone do not
suffice to discharge the regulatory duties of traceability, threat
modelling, data governance, supply-chain integrity, and operational
observability.
4.1
Code-development rules
The four non-negotiable code rules are:
•
CR-1.
No dead code.
“Dead code” is code whose removal does
not affect the program’s behaviour. No function, class, or branch
ships without a test or an executable docstring example reaching it.
A test suite
test_docstring_examples_*.py
enforces this.
•
CR-2.
Deterministic transformations.
Same input, same
bit-level output. Random-number generators are seeded; dictionary
iteration order is not relied upon;
joblib
parallelism is
pinned to a fixed
n_jobs
argument. No generation of random
numbers without a seed and no dependence on Python dictionary
iteration orders.
•
CR-3.
Fail-safe handling.
Invalid inputs raise an explicit
exception rather than being silently imputed or coerced. For example,
NaN values, wrong dtypes, or missing indices raise explicit
ValueError
or
TypeError
exceptions. Silent
imputation is available only behind a typed
Literal["raise", "ffill_bfill", "passthrough"]
switch that must be set explicitly.
•
CR-4.
Minimal CVE attack surface (Common Vulnerabilities and
Exposures).
A short, versioned blocklist (“negative list”) of
forbidden dependencies (
plotly
,
matplotlib
,
spotoptim
,
optuna
,
torch
,
tensorflow
) is implemented by a test that inspects the
lock files with
grep
.
These rules at the same time deliver the secure-software development
practices required by IEC 62443-4-1
[
41
]
. The standard
defines eight process areas
10
10
10
Security Management (SM),
Specification of Security Requirements (SR), Secure by Design (SD),
Secure Implementation (SI), Security Verification and Validation
(SVV), Management of Security-related Issues (DM), Security Update
Management (SUM), and Security Guidelines (SG).
on four maturity
levels (Maturity Level, ML 1
Initial
to ML 4
Improving
).
The rule “no dead code” instantiates SM-1 (security-management
process) and SI-1 (enforced coding guidelines); “deterministic
transformations” instantiates SVV-1 and SVV-3 (reproducible
verification); “fail-safe handling” instantiates SR-2
(threat-model-driven requirements) and DM-1 (defect detection); the
minimal-scope rule instantiates SM-7 (security of third-party
components). The maturity level of the Security Development Lifecycle
(SDL) currently demonstrated by the package is ML 2
Managed
.
The maturity level ML 3
Defined
requires a formal threat model,
which is listed among the future work in
Chapter
8
.
4.2
Process rules
Four further rules govern how the package is developed, shipped, and
operated. None of them can be enforced by a pytest assertion against
the source code; each is fulfilled by an artefact
(
MODEL_CARD.md
, a CI workflow, the Semantic-Release
pipeline, or a logging schema) and inspected at audit or release
time. This split corresponds to the six-step iterative
trustworthiness-assurance
process proposed by
[
76
]
,
in which AI-specific regulatory requirements are refined into
verifiable properties whose evidence is collected along the entire
development lifecycle.
•
PR-1.
Traceability.
Every public symbol and every non-trivial
private helper points back to a documented requirement in
MODEL_CARD.md
(Sections 2 to 5) or to a specific clause in
Table
LABEL:tbl-compliance-en
. This satisfies IEC 61508-3
Section 7.2 (specification of software-safety requirements)
[
46
]
, ISO 26262-6 Section 7.4.2 (ASIL-dependent
derivation of requirements)
[
50
]
, and Art. 9 of the
EU AI Act (risk management across the entire lifecycle)
[
24
]
. Orphan symbols — those without a requirement
reference and without a
MODEL_CARD
entry — are flagged
in code review rather than by the CI.
•
PR-2.
Documented threat model.
For every network-facing
module (
downloader/entsoe.py
,
weather/weather_client.py
) a STRIDE table
11
11
11
STRIDE
is a threat-classification scheme introduced by Microsoft. The
acronym stands for
spoofing
(impersonation),
tampering
(manipulation),
repudiation
(deniability),
information disclosure
,
denial of service
, and
elevation of privilege
.
A STRIDE table enumerates, per module and data flow, which of
these six categories applies, which countermeasure is in place,
and in which source file that countermeasure is realised.
is
maintained; every change to the attack surface requires an updated
threat entry in the same pull request. This satisfies IEC 62443-4-1
SR-1 and SR-2 (threat-model-driven security requirements)
[
41
]
and closes the
ML-2-
→
\rightarrow
-ML-3 gap acknowledged in
Chapter
8
.
•
PR-3.
Signed supply chain.
Every release ships a signed PyPI
wheel and a signed software-bill-of-materials attestation (Software
Bill of Materials, SBOM), both published together with the GitHub
release in the
sigstore
transparency log. The signed wheel
satisfies IEC 62443-4-1 SUM-3 (secure delivery of updates)
[
41
]
together with the corresponding delivery-integrity
requirement under Annex I Part I CRA
[
25
]
. The signed
SBOM attestation satisfies Annex I Part II CRA (vulnerability-handling
duties), whose item 1 explicitly mandates the creation of a software
bill of materials. The inbound side of the supply chain is already
covered by code rule 4.
•
PR-4.
Structured audit log.
Every operational action emits a
log record according to a fixed JSON schema under
~/spotforecast2_safe_models/logs/
; the
schema is versioned, and any change triggers a major version bump
via the Conventional-Commits subject
feat!:
. This satisfies
IEC 62443-4-2 SAR 6.1 (accessibility of the audit log)
[
42
]
together with IEC 61508-3 Section 7.6 (procedures
for software operation and modification)
[
46
]
, both of
which require operational traceability beyond the
development-accompanying tests.
A further, separately maintained rule concerns data governance: the
package itself ships no training data; every forecaster object,
however, records the provenance of the data on which it was fitted
(source URL, retrieval timestamp, content hash) in its persisted
state. This satisfies the provenance requirement of Art. 10 of the
EU AI Act (data and data governance)
[
24
]
, and thus as
much as a library can satisfy without insight into the deployer’s
actual training data. The assessment of representativeness, bias, and
fitness for the intended purpose remains the responsibility of the
deployer and is documented in the deployer’s own
MODEL_CARD.md
. We treat this duty as a refinement of the
traceability rule rather than as a fifth process rule, because the
mechanism (provenance as a persisted artefact) is a special case of
the requirement-to-implementation linkage.
A further principle that does not strictly belong to the four rules
but follows from them is that every source file carries an SPDX header
in the format recommended by REUSE v3.0
[
29
]
. Ported
skforecast
files carry both the original BSD-3-Clause notice
and the added AGPL-3.0-or-later statement, so that the licence-law
resolution yields a dual-licence derivation rather than reading as an
attempt to relicense third-party software.

5.1
Quality
5.1.1
Fail-safe NaN handling
Every tabular measurement may be missing — because a weather station
reports no data for an hour, because a power meter has been restarted,
or because an import error left a cell empty. In Python such a gap is
marked by the special value
NaN
(“Not a Number”),
analogous to a blank field on a questionnaire that has not yet been
answered. What matters for downstream processing is how the next-stage
software reacts to such a field: should it estimate the empty value
and continue, or should it halt and demand a decision from the
deployer?
The Python time-series libraries established in this space mostly
follow the pattern of “silently keep going”.
pandas
[
68
]
interpolates missing values linearly between
neighbours via
Series.interpolate()
and leaves leading and
trailing gaps untouched, without warning the caller;
scikit-learn
[
72
]
replaces NaN by the column mean
by default through the
SimpleImputer
; the forecasting
package
skforecast
[
2
]
that builds on top of it
propagates remaining NaN through to the forecast, where they mix
with the regular model error;
Darts
[
33
]
and
statsmodels
follow the same line. For exploratory work this
stance is convenient — it permits rapid prototyping with messy
data. For deployment in safety-critical environments, however, e.g. electric load forecasting for a grid operator, it is exactly the case
that Art. 15 of the EU AI Act, IEC 61508, and ISO 26262 seek to avoid:
an operator may dispatch on the basis of an apparently complete
forecast without noticing that part of the input data was missing and
was substituted by the package without enquiry.
A concrete example makes the difference clear: suppose a grid
dispatcher is to forecast hourly load for the coming day, and the
weather API returns no temperature value for a single hour — say
03:00. Under the silent approach, the forecasting package would
interpolate the value linearly between 02:00 and 04:00, return the
load forecast with an apparently normal confidence interval, and the
dispatcher would schedule the 03:00 hour as usual. The gap in the
measurements would be visible only in the preprocessing log — and
only if someone takes the trouble to read it. Under the fail-safe
approach, by contrast, the transformer aborts with an explicit error
message, the continuous-integration pipeline raises an alarm, and the
deployer must take a documented decision: switch to a fallback model,
flag the 03:00 hour as invalid, or close the gap deliberately and
record that decision in the audit log.
spotforecast2-safe
inverts the default of the libraries
listed above. The transformer
LinearlyInterpolateTS
accepts
a keyword
on_missing
that takes only three values, and the
default is
"raise"
:
from
spotforecast2_safe.preprocessing
import
LinearlyInterpolateTS
# Raises ValueError if NaN values remain after interpolation:
t
=
LinearlyInterpolateTS(on_missing
=
"raise"
)
# Explicit opt-in to forward/backward fill of the endpoints:
t
=
LinearlyInterpolateTS(on_missing
=
"ffill_bfill"
)
# The caller commits to handling NaN downstream:
t
=
LinearlyInterpolateTS(on_missing
=
"passthrough"
)
The abort under
"raise"
is the desired outcome, not a fault:
the error becomes visible, reaches the CI pipeline, and forces the
deployer to take an explicit decision. The central point is that the
deployer cannot, in an audit, claim to have overlooked the data gap
— because the package actively reported it. The mechanism therefore
addresses Art. 15 of the EU AI Act (accuracy and robustness)
[
24
]
, the fail-safe principle of IEC 61508-3
[
46
]
, and ISO 26262-6 §8.4.5 (prohibition of silent
zero-defaults)
[
50
]
directly.
An executable docstring test enforces that every
Literal
value produces the documented behaviour.
5.2
Testing
5.2.1
Continuous integration
Continuous integration runs entirely via GitHub Actions; the status
badges of the workflows
ci.yml
(test suite) and
docs.yml
(Quarto rendering of the documentation) tell a
downstream user, before installation, whether the tests and the
documentation build pass on the main branch.
5.2.2
Code coverage
The line coverage of the test suite is computed by
Codecov
12
12
12
https://codecov.io
on every push and exposed
as a badge; this makes the minimum threshold of 80 % line coverage
for new code, fixed in
CONTRIBUTING.md
and
pyproject.toml
, publicly verifiable.
5.2.3
SPDX and REUSE conformance
SPDX
(Software Package Data Exchange) is a standardised,
machine-readable format for licence and copyright metadata in source
files; the specification was developed by the Linux Foundation and
ratified in 2021 as the standard ISO/IEC 5962:2021
[
48
]
.
SPDX header lines such as
SPDX-FileCopyrightText
and
SPDX-License-Identifier
allow a software-bill-of-materials
(SBOM) toolchain to reconstruct a package’s licence situation from
the sources without manual inspection. The
REUSE Specification
of the Free Software Foundation Europe, currently in version 3.0
[
29
]
, operationalises SPDX into three verifiable
requirements: (i) every file carries an
SPDX-FileCopyrightText
and an
SPDX-License-Identifier
entry in the header; (ii) the full
texts of all licences used in the project are placed in the directory
LICENSES/
; (iii) compliance is verified automatically by the
tool
reuse lint
. A project is
REUSE-conformant
exactly when
reuse lint
runs without findings. The licence
traceability that results addresses the provenance-documentation
duties of Art. 10 of the EU AI Act (data governance)
[
24
]
and supports the SM (security management) practice of IEC 62443-4-1
(inventory of third-party components)
[
41
]
.
Every source file carries an SPDX header. Ported
skforecast
modules carry a dual header:
# SPDX-FileCopyrightText: skforecast team
# SPDX-FileCopyrightText: 2026 bartzbeielstein
# SPDX-License-Identifier: AGPL-3.0-or-later AND BSD-3-Clause
uv run reuse lint
runs without findings on every commit;
the file
.reuse/dep5
records the small number of data
fixtures whose licence is declared externally. The
REUSE
status
badge in the
README.md
exposes this lint result
publicly and makes the licence conformance assessable without
locally cloning the repository.
5.3
Scores
5.3.1
OpenSSF Best Practices Badge
The Open Source Security Foundation (OpenSSF)
13
13
13
https://openssf.org
maintains two independent programmes for assessing the security
maturity of open-source projects. The
OpenSSF Best Practices
Badge
programme
14
14
14
https://www.bestpractices.dev
inspects project-side submitted evidence on documentation, source
accessibility, security reporting channels, dependency management,
and test and build automation against the maturity levels
passing
,
silver
, and
gold
.
spotforecast2-safe
is registered under project ID 11932 and
demonstrates the
passing
maturity level.
5.3.2
OpenSSF Scorecard
The
OpenSSF Scorecard
15
15
15
https://scorecard.dev
complements this self-attestation programme with an automated,
weekly-updated assessment of the project supply chain against a fixed
list of check points, including branch protection, token scoping, use
of Dependabot, static security analysis, and signed releases. The
assessment is produced by the workflow
scorecard.yml
and is
recorded in Table
6
on the DM row as an
early-warning signal.
5.4
Status
The remaining badges of the
Status
group — maintenance
activity and a fixed source-code formatter
(
black
16
16
16
https://github.com/psf/black
) — do
not constitute a conformance demonstration in the sense of the
EU AI Act or IEC 62443. They do, however, make visible that the
package is actively maintained and that its source code is subject
to a deterministic style formatter, which together with
ruff
,
isort
, and
mypy
is already anchored
in Chapter
4
as part of the code-development
rules.
5.5
Quarantining of corrupted caches
The weather-data client stores API responses as Parquet files in a
configurable directory. The cache reader catches
(OSError, ValueError)
errors, writes a message at
WARNING
level, and renames the corrupted file to
<cache>.corrupt-<epoch>
so that an operator can recover it forensically. A missing cache file
remains silent and returns
None
, since this is the expected
steady state on a cold start. The concrete implementation is in
src/spotforecast2_safe/weather/weather_client.py
in the
class
WeatherService
: the method
_load_cache()
contains the
(OSError, ValueError)
catch and delegates to
_quarantine_corrupt_cache()
, which renames the cache file
to
<cache>.corrupt-<epoch>
;
the counterpart writes in
_save_cache()
. The behaviour is
covered by
tests/test_weather_client.py::TestWeatherServiceCache
.
5.6
CPE identifier for SBOM integration
A
Software Bill of Materials
(SBOM) is a machine-readable list
of all components of a software package, comparable to the
ingredients list on a food package. It enumerates which third-party
libraries, in which versions, and under which licences are contained
in the shipped software. Tools such as
syft
and
cyclonedx-py
generate an SBOM automatically from a package
and encode it in standardised formats (SPDX
[
48
]
or
CycloneDX). On this basis a deployer can determine, without source-code
analysis, which components are present in a given installation —
the prerequisite for any subsequent vulnerability assessment.
A
Common Platform Enumeration
(CPE) is the standardised
identifier under which a single software product appears in such a
bill of materials, comparable to a standardised barcode. The CPE 2.3
specification of NIST
[
11
]
prescribes a fixed format:
cpe:2.3:a:<vendor>:<product>:<version>:...
.
A typical identifier for the present package is therefore:
cpe:2.3:a:bartzbeielstein:spotforecast2-safe:1.0.0:*:*:*:*:python:*:*
.
With a stable CPE identifier, a vulnerability scanner (e.g. Trivy
or Grype) can determine automatically that a particular version of a
product is affected as soon as a CVE is reported under the same CPE
— in particular through the catalogue of Known Exploited
Vulnerabilities (KEV) maintained by the U.S. Cybersecurity and
Infrastructure Security Agency (CISA)
[
16
]
. Without a
fixed CPE, scanners would have to map the package name to a CPE
value via local heuristics; that works for unambiguously named
packages but yields false positives in the case of name collisions
or renamings, or leaves real vulnerabilities undetected.
The distinguishing feature of
spotforecast2-safe
is that the
CPE identifier is not guessed by downstream tools but is fixed
within the package itself. Most Python packages declare no CPE and
rely on the heuristics of scanners such as
pip-audit
or
safety
. By contrast, the file
src/spotforecast2_safe/utils/cpe.py
contains the canonical
CPE string, and
tests/test_cpe.py
verifies it in a
round-trip (serialisation
→
\to
deserialisation
→
\to
identical
object). This elevates the identifier to a first-class API contract:
a breaking change — e.g. a switch of the vendor string or product
name — requires a Conventional-Commits subject
feat!:
,
which forces a major version bump via
semantic-release
and
appears as an entry in the
CHANGELOG.md
. A deployer can
therefore rely on the CPE listed in their SBOM remaining stable
across the entire minor-version line.
From a regulatory perspective, this approach matches the three duties
that the fixed CPE directly serves: Annex I Part II No. 1 CRA
[
25
]
requires the manufacturer of a product with digital
elements to produce a software bill of materials; Art. 72 of the
EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges providers of high-risk AI systems
to perform post-market monitoring, which presupposes a reliable
attribution of CVE reports to the own product; and the process area
SM
(Security Management) of IEC 62443-4-1
[
41
]
requires an inventory of third-party components, which in this
mechanism is the SBOM. The concrete benefit: a deployer no longer has
to rely on a scanner’s guess as to which of their installed packages
a particular CVE applies to, but can derive the affectedness
deterministically from the CPE fixed in the package.
5.7
ISA/IEC 62443 and the CPS security context
5.7.1
The 62443 standards series in
overview
The ISA/IEC 62443 standards family is organised into four groups:
1.
General
(Part 1 — terminology and models
[
39
]
),
2.
Policies and procedures
(Part 2 — programmes for asset
owners
[
45
]
and service providers
[
44
]
),
3.
System
(Part 3 — risk assessment
[
43
]
and
security requirements at the system level
[
40
]
),
and
4.
Component
(Part 4 — secure product development
[
41
]
and technical requirements for components
[
42
]
).
In the taxonomy of 62443-4-2, a time-series forecasting library is a
Software Application
(alongside
Embedded Device
,
Host Device
, and
Network Device
); it therefore inherits
the Software-Application-Requirements (SAR) subset of the component
catalogue rather than the full Embedded Device profile.
Table
4
summarises this organisation.
Tabelle 4:
Structure of the ISA/IEC 62443
standards series along the four groups “General”, “Policies and
procedures”, “System”, and “Component”. The organisation follows
the role of the addressee (asset owner, service provider, system
integrator, component manufacturer) and not the Purdue levels; the
heuristic mapping to Purdue levels is summarised in
Table
5
. In the component taxonomy of
62443-4-2, a forecasting library is a “Software Application”
(alongside “Embedded Device”, “Host Device”, and “Network
Device”) and therefore inherits the Software-Application-Requirements
(SAR) subset. CSMS = Cyber Security Management System; SDL = Security
Development Lifecycle; SAR = Software Application Requirements;
SR = Security Requirements; SL = Security Level.
Group
Part
Title and addressee
General
62443-1-1
Terminology and models (cross-cutting)
Policies and procedures
62443-2-1
Programmes for asset owners (CSMS)
62443-2-4
Requirements for service providers
System
62443-3-2
Risk assessment and system design
62443-3-3
Security requirements at the system level (SR, SL)
Component
62443-4-1
Secure product-development lifecycle (SDL)
62443-4-2
Technical requirements for components
NIST SP 800-82r3
[
77
]
presents the Purdue model, the
ISA/IEC 62443 view, and the Industrial-Internet-Consortium (IIC) tier
model side by side as three
alternative
segmentation
frameworks; Figure
3
reproduces this
juxtaposition. The 62443 view there uses
zones and conduits
(Enterprise
→
\to
Conduit
→
\to
DMZ
→
\to
Control Center
→
\to
Conduit
→
\to
Basic Process Control System (BPCS)
→
\to
Conduit
→
\to
Safety Instrumented System (SIS)) instead of Purdue
levels 0–5. The 62443 series therefore does
not
map 1:1 onto
Purdue levels, because its parts are organised by
role
—
asset owner, service provider, system integrator, component
manufacturer — not by network levels. Industrial practice
nevertheless commonly uses a mapping to the Purdue level that follows
the scope of the 62443 parts, because it eases reviewers’ and
auditors’ mental bridge between network segmentation and standardised
requirements. Table
5
summarises this
heuristic mapping; 62443-1-1
[
39
]
(terminology and
models) applies cross-cuttingly at all levels.
Abbildung 3:
Three alternative segmentation
frameworks for industrial automation and control systems: Purdue
reference model with DMZ buffer zones (left), ISA/IEC 62443
zones-and-conduits depiction (middle), and Industrial Internet
Consortium tier model (right). Source: NIST SP 800-82r3, Fig. 16
[
77
]
, p. 72; work of an officer of the U.S. federal
government and therefore in the public domain under 17 U.S.C. § 105
(republished courtesy of the National Institute of Standards and
Technology). BPCS = Basic Process Control System; SIS = Safety
Instrumented System; DMZ = Demilitarized Zone; IIC = Industrial
Internet Consortium.
Tabelle 5:
Heuristic mapping of the
ISA/IEC 62443 parts to the levels of the Purdue model. The mapping
follows the scope of the parts (component development, system
integration, asset-owner programme) and is not a normative
prescription; 62443-1-1 (terminology and models) is cross-cuttingly
valid. The IT/OT DMZ is not a Purdue level in the strict sense, but
was added in the NIST “Purdue with DMZ” variant as a buffer zone at
the IT/OT boundary between levels 3 and 4 (informally also called
level 3.5). IT = Information Technology; OT = Operational Technology;
DMZ = Demilitarized Zone; CSMS = Cyber Security Management System;
SR = Security Requirements; SL = Security Level; SDL = Security
Development Lifecycle.
Purdue level
Primarily applicable 62443 parts
Rationale
Level 5 — Enterprise
(IT)
62443-2-1

≈
\approx
ISO 27001 for CPS)
Level 4 — Business planning
(IT)
62443-2-1, 62443-2-4
Asset-owner programme and service-provider requirements

(boundary)
62443-3-2
Zones-and-conduits concept
Level 3 — Manufacturing operations management
(OT)
62443-3-2, 62443-3-3
System level: risk assessment, SR, SL
Level 2 — Area control
(OT)
62443-3-3, 62443-4-2
System SR and component profile
Level 1 — Basic control
(OT)
62443-4-1, 62443-4-2
Component manufacturer: secure SDL and technical requirements

(OT)
62443-4-2
Sensors and actuators as
embedded devices
(62443-4-2 profile)
5.7.2
Secure product-development lifecycle
(62443-4-1)
IEC 62443-4-1 builds on Microsoft’s Security Development Lifecycle
(SDL)
[
36
]
. SDL describes a process in which every
development phase (requirements, design, implementation, verification,
release, response) is paired with an explicit security activity such
as threat modelling, static analysis, fuzz testing, and incident
response. SDL defines eight process areas on four maturity levels.
Table
6
reports the current evidence status for
each practice in
spotforecast2-safe
. The package reaches
ML 2
Managed
across all eight practices; ML 3
Defined
requires a formal threat model and a documented security-assessment
report, which are tracked in the M1–M7 backlog of
REVIEW_FINDINGS.md
.
5.7.3
Foundational requirements and security levels (62443-3-3
and 62443-4-2)
IEC 62443-3-3 and 62443-4-2 share seven foundational requirements
(Foundational Requirements: FR 1 identification and authentication
control, FR 2 use control, FR 3 system integrity, FR 4 data
confidentiality, FR 5 restricted data flow, FR 6 timely response to
events, FR 7 resource availability) and four security levels
(Security Level: SL 1 protection against casual or coincidental
violation up to SL 4 protection against intentional attacks by actors
with extended resources).
A pure software library cannot, on its own, satisfy requirements that
depend on the surrounding system (network segmentation (FR 5) and
authentication (FR 1) are the clearest examples), but it can avoid
breaking
those requirements, and that is exactly the stance
that
spotforecast2-safe
adopts. FR 3 (system integrity) is
supported by deterministic transformations, pinned dependencies, and
SPDX-tracked provenance; FR 6 (timely response to events) is
supported by tight exception handling and structured logging
(Chapter
5
); FR 7 (resource availability) is
supported by the minimal-scope rule, which caps the transitive
dependency closure. The library therefore supports an SL 2 system
when it is itself embedded into an SL 2 zone; the security level is
a property of the zone, not of the library in isolation.
Tabelle 6:
Current mapping of the package to
the eight process areas of IEC 62443-4-1 Section 5.5. The ML columns
indicate the demonstrated maturity level, not an external
certification.
Practice
Required evidence
Package evidence
ML
SM (Security Management)
Documented security roles; inventory of third-party components; personnel guidelines.
CONTRIBUTING.md
;
CODEOWNERS
; pinned
uv.lock
; SPDX headers in every source file.
2
SR (Security Requirements)
Threat-driven security requirements; typed API contracts.
Fail-safe
on_missing
contract; typed
Literal
switches in the preprocessing layer;

); blocklist of forbidden dependencies; tight exception handling at the network boundary.

2
5.7.4
CPS specifics that shape the software
design
[
27
]
and
[
28
]
highlight four specifics of CPS
cybersecurity that distinguish it from classical IT security and that
shape the design of the present package:
1.
Long lifecycles.
Industrial plants are operated for 20 to
40 years; a forecasting model shipped today may still be producing
setpoints in 2050. Deterministic serialisation, a pinned public API,
and a CPE identifier are necessary preconditions for retrospective
verifiability across that horizon.
2.
Convergence of safety and security.
A forecast that drives a
physical actuator is a safety-functional input. The technical report
IEC TR 63069 explicitly addresses the joint safety-and-security
problem; the package adopts its conclusion that safety decisions,
once taken, must not be overwritten by a later security fix —
hence the fail-safe default “exception on invalid input” rather
than a silent data correction.
3.
Late security integration as a dominant root cause of
failure.
Interviews with engineers from INEOS and HIMA reported by
[
28
]
show that security is typically appended after the
basic-engineering phase, i.e. once the design-time-favourable
decisions (network topology, component selection) have already been
made. A library that offers explicit error paths and
machine-readable provenance is cheaper to integrate early than one
that hides state.
4.
Interdisciplinary authorship.
[
28
]
(REQ 2.2.1)
requires that security decisions be accessible to non-security
experts. The package’s executable docstrings, typed contracts, and
file-based audit logs fulfil exactly this role for the process
engineer who takes over a fitted forecaster without being a Python
or security specialist.
The principle behind point 4 is developed further by
[
26
]
as a
Security Decision Base
: a structured notation for
security-relevant information that satisfies six requirements —
support of human decision-making (R1), accessibility for automation
engineers (R2), ICS specificity (R3), method independence (R4),
traceability of the decision itself (R5), and reusability (R6). The
process rules 1 (traceability) and 2 (documented threat model) from
Chapter
4
are the
spotforecast2-safe
instantiation of R5 and R3 for the
narrower case of a Python forecasting library.
VDI/VDE 2182-1
[
79
]
provides a procedural model for
operationalising these principles in the German industrial-automation
context.
6
EU AI Act requirements and applicable standards
Building on
[
73
]
,
[
76
]
compile which ISO/IEC and
ETSI standards operationalise the individual requirements of the
EU AI Act for high-risk systems; Table
7
adopts
this inventory. From this inventory a tighter core can be distilled:
[
73
]
compress the twelve operationalisation- and
suitability-essential standards into six so-called
core
standards
(Figure
4
) that are present in
both essential groups and therefore form the smallest common
denominator of the maturity and operationalisation analysis carried
out. Concretely, these are the following six standards: ISO/IEC
4213, ISO/IEC 5338, ISO/IEC 23894-2, ISO/IEC 24027, ISO/IEC 38507, and
ISO/IEC 42001. Table
LABEL:tbl-core-standards-en
explains the six
core standards row by row and refers in each case to the ISO/IEC
primary source.
