6.1
EU AI Act and ISO/IEC 25059
The code and process rules of Chapter
4
map
onto individual provisions of the EU AI Act, IEC 61508-3,
ISO 26262-6, the ISA/IEC 62443 standards series, and the CRA.
6.1.1
Article 4 of the EU AI Act (AI literacy)
Article 4 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges providers and
deployers to ensure that their staff and other persons operating or
using an AI system on their behalf possess a sufficient level of AI
literacy, calibrated to the use context and to the groups of persons
on which the system acts.
spotforecast2-safe
is designed so
that a process engineer without formal training in machine learning
can attain that threshold by reading the package alone. Three
mechanisms contribute. First, every public transformer and every
forecaster carries executable docstring examples, so that the
intended behaviour can be learnt by running small, self-contained
snippets and is not dependent on the study of external manuals.
Second, typed contracts make the pre- and post-conditions of every
call visible at the call site; this lowers the expert knowledge
required to read a training script correctly. Third, the structured
audit log (rule 4 in Chapter
4
) discloses, line
by line, the sequence of decisions taken by a running forecaster, so
that the deployer’s staff can review the operational behaviour after
the fact. The model-related
MODEL_CARD.md
complements
these mechanisms by recording the intended deployment frame and the
known limits in a language with which non-specialist operating
personnel can also work. Taken together, these design choices lower
the AI-literacy threshold the deployer must reach in order to
supervise the system responsibly, and thereby support the obligation
of Art. 4 of the EU AI Act directly.
6.1.2
Article 9 of the EU AI Act (risk management)
Article 9 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges the provider of a
high-risk AI system to establish, implement, document, and maintain a
risk-management system (Risk Management System, RMS) (paragraph 1).
This is to be understood as a continuous, iterative process across
the entire lifecycle and comprises four steps (paragraph 2):
identification and analysis of known and reasonably foreseeable risks
(Art. 9(2)(a) of the EU AI Act); estimation and evaluation of risks
in the intended use and under reasonably foreseeable misuse
(Art. 9(2)(b) of the EU AI Act); evaluation of further risks from the
post-market monitoring system under Art. 72 of the EU AI Act
(Art. 9(2)(c) of the EU AI Act); and the adoption of suitable,
targeted risk-management measures (Art. 9(2)(d) of the EU AI Act).
The measures must take into account the combined interplay of the
requirements of Chapter III Section 2 (paragraph 4) and must be such
that the residual risks remaining are acceptable (paragraph 5). The
system must be tested against pre-defined metrics and probabilistic
thresholds (paragraphs 6 and 8), in particular with regard to minors
and other vulnerable groups (paragraph 9); under parallel regulation
it may be combined with existing Union risk-management processes
(paragraph 10).
For a forecasting library embedded as a component, the same shared
responsibility described in Chapter
6.1.3
and
Chapter
6.1.12
recurs: the provider of the embedded
high-risk system remains the primary addressee of the RMS duty.
spotforecast2-safe
, however, covers the two
provider-side duties that a library can address on its own: the
risk-mitigating design of its own code (Art. 9(3) and Art. 9(5)(a) of
the EU AI Act) and the supply of the technical information on which
the provider builds its own RMS (Art. 9(5)(c) of the EU AI Act).
Three mechanisms carry this work.
1.
Threat-model-based risk identification
(Art. 9(2)(a) and (b) of the EU AI Act). For every network-facing
module (
downloader/entsoe.py
,
weather/weather_client.py
) a STRIDE table is maintained
in
CONTRIBUTING.md
; every change of the attack surface
requires, via the pull-request template, an updated threat entry
in the same commit. This represents the identification and
evaluation steps for the intended use and reasonably foreseeable
misuse of the network-mediated part of the library. For the
data-side part, the typed
on_missing
contract (see
Chapter
6.1.3
) acts as misuse early-warning: a
missing or non-numerical input is not silently imputed but raised
to a documentation-required exception.
2.
Design-side risk mitigation
(Art. 9(2)(d) and Art. 9(5)(a)
and (b) of the EU AI Act). The four code rules of
Chapter
4
(CR-1
No dead code
, CR-2
Determinism
, CR-3
Fail-safe
, CR-4
Minimal CVE
attack surface
) and the four process rules (PR-1
Traceability
, PR-2
Threat model
, PR-3
Signed
supply chain
, PR-4
Structured audit log
) are the concrete
risk-management measures anchored in source code and the
development pipeline. They eliminate certain classes of risk
through the design of the library (Art. 9(5)(a) of the EU AI Act)
rather than through downstream usage notices, and are, in the
sense of Art. 9(4) of the EU AI Act, explicitly aligned with the
interplay of the remaining requirements of Chapter III Section 2:
the mapping is laid out row by row in
Table
LABEL:tbl-compliance-en
and summarised visually in
Figure
5
.
3.
Continuous testing and feedback from monitoring
(Art. 9(2)(c) and Art. 9(6) and (8) of the EU AI Act). The log
instrumentation described in Chapter
6.1.12
feeds
events observed after placing on the market back into risk
analysis; the executable docstrings run via
tests/test_docstring_examples_*.py
, the minimum
threshold of 80 % line coverage fixed in
pyproject.toml
and the rolling-origin cross-validation
split_ts_cv
together form the test layer required by Art. 9(6) and (8) against
pre-defined metrics. CodeQL, Scorecard, and Dependabot extend that
test layer with security-oriented checks across the whole
lifecycle.
Article 9(9) of the EU AI Act (adverse impact on minors and other
vulnerable groups) does not directly apply to the typical use case
of electric load forecasting, because the forecast result does not
carry decisions about individual persons; the assessment in the
concrete deployment context nevertheless remains the provider’s
task. Article 9(10) of the EU AI Act allows a provider already
subject to another Union risk-management regime (e.g. IEC 62443-4-1
SM-1 as part of CRA harmonisation, or an NIS-2 information-security
management system) to integrate the EU AI Act duties into the
existing procedure. The mapping to IEC 62443-4-1 is laid out in
Table
6
and supports precisely this dual use:
the maturity level ML 2
Managed
certified there confirms a
documented risk-management process whose output the provider’s
EU-AI-Act RMS can dock onto directly.
6.1.3
Article 10 of the EU AI Act (data governance)
Article 10 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges the provider of
a high-risk AI system to data-governance measures across training,
validation, and test datasets (paragraphs 1–4) and contains an
exception rule for special categories of personal data (paragraph 5)
together with a scope limitation for pure inference systems
(paragraph 6). For a forecasting library that does not itself ship
training data, this implies a shared responsibility: the deployer
remains the primary addressee of the substantive requirements; the
package provides the technical infrastructure that allows the
deployer to satisfy them and prevents them from being silently
bypassed. Three mechanisms carry this support.
1.
Documented design choices and assumptions
(Art. 10(2)(a)
and (d) of the EU AI Act). The structured
MODEL_CARD.md
framework (see Chapter
6.1.4
), with sections
Uses
,
Bias, Risks, and Limitations
,
Evaluation
, and
Environmental Impact
, forces the
deployer to record the relevant design choices and the
assumptions about the input data in writing.
2.
Data provenance and preprocessing
(Art. 10(2)(b) and (c)
of the EU AI Act). Every
Forecaster
object persists in
its state the provenance of the data on which it was fitted —
source URL, retrieval timestamp, and content hash. For
live-data paths, the adapters
downloader/entsoe.py
for
ENTSO-E
[
18
]
and
weather/weather_client.py
for Open-Meteo
[
80
]
are available; both record the
retrieval parameters in the persisted state. The only shipped
preprocessing with data-changing semantics — linear
interpolation to fill missing timestamps — is encapsulated by
the typed
on_missing
switch (see
Chapter
4.1
, CR-3) so that it does not take
effect silently but only after an explicit opt-in by the
deployer. Annotation and labelling do not arise in the
regression-based forecasting use case.
3.
Detection of data gaps
(Art. 10(2)(h) of the EU AI Act).
The fail-safe default
on_missing="raise"
turns a
silently-bypassed data gap into a discoverable, documentation-
required event. The deployer is forced to identify the gap as
such and to take a documented decision (model fallback, marking
as invalid, or explicit opt-in to interpolation). This
operationalises the “identification of relevant data gaps or
shortcomings” required by Art. 10(2)(h) of the EU AI Act.
For the bias requirements (Art. 10(2)(f) and (g) of the EU AI Act)
and the representativeness duty (Art. 10(3) of the EU AI Act),
assessment lies with the deployer, because biases arise only from
the interplay of training data and deployment scenario; the library
supports verifiability through deterministic transformations
(CR-2, see Chapter
6.1.8
), so that repeated
experiments on the same dataset deliver bit-identical results and
deviations can be attributed exclusively to data differences. The
representativeness can be checked empirically by the deployer with
the ported rolling-origin cross-validation
split_ts_cv
.
Special categories of personal data (Art. 10(5) of the EU AI Act)
do not arise in the typical load-forecasting use case. If the
package is used exclusively for inference with an already-trained
model, the scope of Art. 10(2) to (5) of the EU AI Act narrows
under Art. 10(6) of the EU AI Act to the test dataset. This is a
configuration that the
Forecaster
persistence mechanism
expressly supports through content hashes of the training data.
6.1.4
Article 11 of the EU AI Act (technical
documentation)
The open-source tool
quartodoc
[
14
]
generates a
searchable HTML API reference from a Python library’s docstrings
inside the Quarto publication system
[
1
]
and provides,
for
spotforecast2-safe
, the link between the executable
docstrings in the source code and the technical documentation
required by Annex IV of the EU AI Act.
Article 11 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
read together with
Annex IV of the EU AI Act requires that, for every high-risk AI
system, technical documentation be drawn up before placing on the
market and kept up to date, enabling a competent authority or a
notified body to perform conformity assessment. For
spotforecast2-safe
, three artefacts carry this duty: the
MODEL_CARD.md
, versioned in the repository, following the
taxonomy of the Hugging Face Model Card Guidebook
[
71
]
with the sections
Uses
,
Bias, Risks, and Limitations
,
How to Get Started
,
Evaluation
,
Environmental
Impact
,
Glossary
,
Citation
and the mapping to the
EU AI Act and IEC 62443; the API reference under
docs/
generated automatically from docstrings via
quartodoc
; and
the present report itself, which records the architectural design
decisions and their mapping to the regulatory provisions. The
narrative documentation complements the machine-verifiable evidence
of Chapter
6.1.6
and Chapter
6.1.8
without replacing it.
6.1.5
Article 12 of the EU AI Act (record-keeping
obligations)
Article 12 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges the provider to
design a high-risk AI system so that it records events (“logs”)
technically and automatically across the entire lifecycle
(paragraph 1). The logging must capture those events (paragraph 2)
that serve to identify risks within the meaning of Art. 79(1) of the
EU AI Act and substantial modifications (Art. 12(2)(a) of the
EU AI Act), enable post-market monitoring under Art. 72 of the
EU AI Act (Art. 12(2)(b) of the EU AI Act), and support the
oversight of systems covered by Art. 26(5) of the EU AI Act
(Art. 12(2)(c) of the EU AI Act). For the special class of remote
biometric identification systems (Annex III No. 1(a) of the
EU AI Act), paragraph 3 additionally requires minimum content —
duration of use, reference database, input data of a hit, and
persons involved.
The implementation in the package is anchored in process rule PR-4
(see Chapter
4.2
) as a structured audit log
and concretely realised in the module
src/spotforecast2_safe/manager/logger.py
. The entry point
is the function
setup_logging()
, which sets up a
double-handler logger: a
console handler
with a plain-text
formatter for human readability (configurable level, default
INFO
) and a
file handler
with the corresponding
class’s
JsonAuditFormatter
, which persists at
INFO
level independently of the console verbosity. The
log file is placed with a timestamped name
(
task_safe_n_to_1_YYYYMMDD_HHMMSS.log
) in the
configurable log directory — by default
~/spotforecast2_safe_models/logs/
—
so that every run produces its own log, sortable by start time.
This satisfies Art. 12(1) of the EU AI Act (automatic logging
across the lifecycle) in a deployer-reproducible form of a
JSON-lines file.
The schema of individual log records is fixed in
src/spotforecast2_safe/manager/audit_log_schema.json
(version 1.0.0) and comprises six mandatory fields:
schema_version
(constant “1.0.0”),
timestamp_utc
(ISO 8601 UTC with microsecond precision
and trailing “Z”),
logger
(Python logger name),
level
(
DEBUG
/
INFO
/
WARNING
/
ERROR
/
CRITICAL
),
event
(short slug such as
task_start
,
fit
,
predict
,
fetch
), and
message
(formatted plain text); in addition there are the
optional fields
task
(name of the originating top-level
task),
context
(structured supplement from
extra={...}
), and
exception
(traceback, if
exc_info
was set). Risk events under Art. 12(2)(a) of the
EU AI Act are identified in this schema by the combination of
level >= ERROR
, a descriptive
event
slug, and the
exception
field; any change
to the schema itself is a
substantial modification
within
the meaning of the provision and is bound by the CI job
audit-log-schema-gate
to a Conventional-Commits subject
feat!:
, which compulsorily triggers a major version bump
via Semantic-Release (see Chapter
6.1.6
). The
traceability of substantial modifications required by
Art. 12(2)(a) of the EU AI Act is thereby mapped from the release
path onto the log schema. The UTC timestamps with microsecond
precision and the constant
schema_version
permit the
deployment-spanning correlation of events for post-market
monitoring (Art. 72 of the EU AI Act) required by Art. 12(2)(b);
the
task
field enables the reconstruction of operational
running required by Art. 12(2)(c).
Article 12(3) of the EU AI Act is not substantively applicable
because
spotforecast2-safe
is a regression-based
forecasting library and not a remote biometric identification
system under Annex III No. 1(a) of the EU AI Act. Should a deployer
wish to record an interval-oriented capture for their own audit
profile, the schema provides a technical basis for it.
6.1.6
Article 13 of the EU AI Act (transparency)
Article 13 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges the provider of
a high-risk AI system to design the system so that the deployer can
interpret and use its output appropriately, and to supply
instructions for use that include, among other things, the intended
purpose, performance characteristics, pre-determined changes, and
necessary maintenance and updating measures. The package treats this
duty as an executable rather than a purely narrative specification;
four mechanisms together form the machine-verifiable part of the
audit trail.
1.
Executable docstrings.
Every public symbol ships with a
docstring whose
>>>
examples are
executed by the test suite via
test_docstring_examples_*.py
on every push. This
rules out a common failure mode in which documentation and
behaviour drift apart unnoticed. Executable docstrings document
the intended purpose and the expected behaviour of every public
symbol and thereby directly address Art. 13(3)(b)(i) of the
EU AI Act (intended purpose) and (vii) (information for the
appropriate interpretation of the output).
2.
Product identity via the CPE identifier.
The CPE 2.3
identifier fixed in the package, described in detail in
Chapter
5.6
[
11
]
, identifies
vendor and product in every derived software bill of materials
(SBOM) and thereby satisfies the identity part of Art. 13(3)(a)
of the EU AI Act; the cybersecurity benefit it provides
(automated resolution against CVE reports and the CISA KEV
catalogue) is discussed in Chapter
6.1.8
alongside the other cybersecurity mechanisms.
3.
Conventional Commits and Semantic-Release.
The commit
message format enforced by pre-commit corresponds to Conventional
Commits. A subject
fix:
leads to a patch version bump,
feat:
to a minor bump,
feat!:
or
fix!:
to a major bump. Semantic-Release reads the commits since the
last tag, computes the next version, overwrites
pyproject.toml
, regenerates the
CHANGELOG.md
,
and publishes both a wheel on PyPI and a GitHub release. The
workflow is attested by the version-controlled GitHub Actions
file
release.yml
. This pipeline addresses Art. 13(3)(c)
of the EU AI Act (pre-determined changes by the provider at the
time of conformity assessment) and Art. 13(3)(e) of the
EU AI Act (software updates as part of maintenance and care
measures); at the same time it operationalises IEC 62443-4-1
SUM-3 (secure delivery of updates)
[
41
]
by
ensuring that every correction reaches consumers via a signed
wheel together with a traceable CHANGELOG entry and a version
bump derivable from the commit history.
4.
Narrative documentation as a complement.
The
machine-verifiable mechanisms (docstrings, CPE,
Semantic-Release) are complemented, not replaced, by narrative
documentation: the present report, the
MODEL_CARD.md
,
and the
CHANGELOG.md
(see Chapter
6.1.4
).
The shipped
MODEL_CARD.md
contains the sections
Uses
,
Bias, Risks, and Limitations
,
How to
Get Started
,
Evaluation
,
Environmental Impact
,
Glossary
, and
Citation
and is therefore itself a
transparency artefact within the meaning of Art. 13 of the
EU AI Act.
6.1.7
Article 14 of the EU AI Act (human
oversight)
The same mechanisms as in Chapter
6.1.1
also address
the provider-side counterpart in Art. 14(4) of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
, according to which a high-risk AI system must be
provided so that the persons entrusted with human oversight are
enabled to understand the capabilities and limits of the system
(Art. 14(4)(a) of the EU AI Act), to monitor its operation and to
detect anomalies, malfunctions, and unexpected performance (also
Art. 14(4)(a) of the EU AI Act), and to interpret its output
correctly (Art. 14(4)(c) of the EU AI Act). Executable docstrings,
typed contracts, and
MODEL_CARD.md
satisfy the
understanding and interpretation duties; the structured audit log
satisfies the monitoring and anomaly-detection duty. Article 4 of
the EU AI Act establishes the deployer-side competence duty;
Article 14(4) of the EU AI Act the provider-side design duty that
makes that competence operationally attainable in the first place.
The artefacts of
spotforecast2-safe
satisfy both duties
from the same source basis.
6.1.8
Article 15 of the EU AI Act (accuracy, robustness, and
cybersecurity)
Article 15 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
describes accuracy,
robustness, and cybersecurity for high-risk AI systems; paragraph 1
requires that these properties be delivered consistently
“throughout their lifecycle”, paragraph 4 demands resilience
against errors, faults, and inconsistencies. On the implementation
side of
spotforecast2-safe
, this consistency requirement
is met through a consistently deterministic execution path secured
on three layers. At the estimator level, LightGBM and XGBoost are
always instantiated with a
random_state
passed through by
the wrapper; in addition, the options
deterministic=True
and
force_col_wise=True
are set for LightGBM to remove
the difference between row-wise and column-wise histogram
construction on multi-core systems. At the feature level, the order
of concatenation of exogenous features is Python-version-stable,
because the
ExogBuilder
stores its components in a list
rather than a dictionary. At the I/O level, a Parquet round-trip
preserves the dtypes but not the frequency of the DatetimeIndex; the
cache reader therefore compares value-based, not frequency-based,
and the dedicated test suite
tests/test_weather_client.py::TestWeatherServiceCache
fixes this behaviour. The consistent performance across the
lifecycle required by Art. 15(1) of the EU AI Act is thus evidenced
in a machine-verifiable, deployer-reproducible form.
In addition to robustness, the cybersecurity requirement under
Art. 15(5) of the EU AI Act addresses the defence and tracking of
vulnerabilities. The CPE 2.3 identifier fixed in the package,
described in detail in Chapter
5.6
[
11
]
, is the technical link that downstream
vulnerability scanners use to assign a CVE reported under the same
CPE deterministically to the installed package version; without
this fixing, scanners would have to rely on heuristic
package-name matches, with the well-known false-positive and
miss risks.
Beyond the binding obligations of the regulation, the AI-specific
extension of the
Systems and software Quality Requirements
and Evaluation
(SQuaRE) standards series, ISO/IEC 25059:2023
[
51
]
, provides the quality vocabulary that the
expected harmonised CEN-CENELEC standards under Art. 40 of the
EU AI Act are likely to adopt. Its quality model refines the
sub-characteristics functional correctness, robustness, and user
controllability of ISO/IEC 25010 for AI systems and is therefore
the natural technical counterpart to Art. 15 of the EU AI Act. The
2023 edition is currently being revised: the Draft International
Standard (DIS) ISO/IEC 25059:2025 is in the survey procedure, and
an EU-AI-Act-aligned European edition (Preliminary European Norm,
prEN ISO/IEC 25059) is expected, via the Joint Technical Committee
21 (JTC 21) of CEN-CENELEC, for public enquiry.
6.1.9
Article 16 of the EU AI Act (provider
duties)
Article 16 of the EU AI Act bundles the provider-side duties for
high-risk AI systems into a catalogue. The catalogue combines
substantive product requirements, organisational requirements on
the provider, and procedural requirements on the placing-on-the-
market and post-market path. To these is added Art. 16(b) of the
EU AI Act, which requires an identifying provider mark on the
product itself, on its packaging, or on the accompanying
documentation.
Because the catalogue refers predominantly to other articles of the
regulation, Art. 16(a) of the EU AI Act is, for
spotforecast2-safe
, satisfied through the sections
already treated on Art. 9 (Chapter
6.1.2
), Art. 10
(Chapter
6.1.3
), Art. 11
(Chapter
6.1.4
), Art. 12
(Chapter
6.1.5
), Art. 13
(Chapter
6.1.6
), and Art. 15
(Chapter
6.1.8
) of the EU AI Act; the duty under
Art. 16(c) of the EU AI Act through Section
Chapter
6.1.10
, that under Art. 16(d) of the
EU AI Act through Section Chapter
6.1.11
, and that
under Art. 16(j) of the EU AI Act through Section
Chapter
6.1.13
. For a forecasting library embedded
as a component, the shared responsibility described in the
preceding sections recurs: the provider of the embedded high-risk
system remains the primary addressee. The package directly covers
three duty clusters at component level.
1.
Identity and contact
(Art. 16(b) of the EU AI Act). The
package name, the AGPL-3.0-or-later licence, the maintainer
address, and the vulnerability reporting channel are fixed in
pyproject.toml
, in the PyPI listing, and in
.github/SECURITY.md
and are republished with every
release. The CPE 2.3 identifier described in
Chapter
5.6
is the machine-readable form of the
same statement and appears in every derived software bill of
materials. A provider that integrates the library into a
high-risk AI system can adopt the provenance information required
by Art. 16(b) of the EU AI Act deterministically from the
package, instead of maintaining it manually.
2.
On-demand evidence
(Art. 16(k) in conjunction with
Art. 16(a) of the EU AI Act). The technical documentation
described in Chapter
6.1.4
(
MODEL_CARD.md
, the
quartodoc
API reference,
and the present report), the row-by-row mapping in
Table
LABEL:tbl-compliance-en
, and the graphical summary in
Figure
5
provide the provider with the
evidence to be presented on official request under Art. 16(k) of
the EU AI Act. Because all evidence is under version control and
reproducible via the signed release tag, the provider can, for
any shipped package version, reconstruct the corresponding
conformity evidence.
3.
Corrections and accessibility of the technical
communication
(Art. 16(j) and Art. 16(l) of the EU AI Act). The
Semantic-Release pipeline described in
Chapter
6.1.6
delivers the corrective measures
required by Art. 16(j) of the EU AI Act in the form of signed,
temporally traceable releases with a corresponding
CHANGELOG.md
entry. For the accessibility of the
technical communication under Art. 16(l) of the EU AI Act, the
API documentation generated with
quartodoc
is shipped
as a pure HTML page with semantic markup (heading levels,
alternative texts on graphics, searchable plain text);
executable docstrings additionally provide the function
description in searchable plain-text form. The package thereby
supports the two accessibility directives (EU) 2016/2102 (public
bodies) and (EU) 2019/882 (European Accessibility Act) at the
documentation level, which a library can reach on its own.
Some duties of Art. 16 of the EU AI Act lie beyond the reach of a
library and remain reserved to the provider: Art. 16(e) of the
EU AI Act concerns the operational retention of the logs
automatically generated by the deployed system (the package
generates these via the audit-log schema, see
Chapter
6.1.5
; their retention falls to the
provider); Art. 16(f) of the EU AI Act requires undergoing a
conformity assessment procedure under Art. 43 of the EU AI Act for
the embedded overall system; Art. 16(g) of the EU AI Act the EU
declaration of conformity under Art. 47 of the EU AI Act; Art. 16(h)
of the EU AI Act the CE marking under Art. 48 of the EU AI Act;
Art. 16(i) of the EU AI Act registration in the EU database under
Art. 49(1) of the EU AI Act. These duties presuppose a complete
product and cannot be satisfied by a component; the
machine-readable conformity evidence described above does, however,
accelerate the provider’s discharging of these procedures.
6.1.10
Article 17 of the EU AI Act (quality-management
system)
The EU AI Act organises the provider-side duties around Art. 17 of
the EU AI Act; this requires a documented quality-management
system (QMS) that integrates the individual substantive duties
(risk management in Art. 9 of the EU AI Act, data governance in
Art. 10 of the EU AI Act, technical documentation in Art. 11 of
the EU AI Act, record-keeping in Art. 12 of the EU AI Act,
transparency in Art. 13 of the EU AI Act, accuracy and robustness
in Art. 15 of the EU AI Act) with the provider-side process duties
(record-keeping of the provider under Art. 18 of the EU AI Act,
post-market monitoring under Art. 72 of the EU AI Act, and
reporting of serious incidents under Art. 73 of the EU AI Act).
Table
LABEL:tbl-compliance-en
therefore contains an “EU AI Act
Art. 17 row” whose mechanism column points to the mechanisms used
to satisfy the respective sub-duty and cross-references the
adjacent rows. A deployer preparing a conformity assessment can
read the “EU AI Act Art. 17 row” as an index into the rest of
the table.
A mechanism not explicitly required by Art. 17 of the EU AI Act,
but indispensable for a software provider’s QMS, is licence
traceability.
spotforecast2-safe
satisfies it through
REUSE v3.0 conformance (detailed in
Chapter
5.2.3
): every source file, every data
fixture, and every test carries an SPDX header;
uv run reuse lint
is integrated into pre-commit and is
run on every commit;
.reuse/dep5
resolves the licence of
the few externally licensed files. From this conformance, the
deployer can at any time generate a
SPDX-3.0-JSON
document via
reuse spdx
and feed it into their own QMS.
Article 13 of the EU AI Act does not itself raise the licence
question; a downstream deployer must, however, clarify it before
integrating the library, and the provider’s QMS supplies the
answer already at release time.
6.1.11
Article 18 of the EU AI Act (retention of
documentation)
The retention obligation of Art. 18 of the EU AI Act primarily
concerns the technical documentation under Art. 11 of the EU AI
Act and the QMS under Art. 17 of the EU AI Act. The market
surveillance authorities competent for it (in Germany the
Bundesnetzagentur) must be able to inspect the high-risk AI system
at any time. The retention period is 10 years after placing on the
market or putting into service (Art. 3 No. 9 and No. 11 of the
EU AI Act, respectively). In view of the forthcoming Product
Liability Act, however, longer retention periods are advisable.
6.1.12
Article 72 of the EU AI Act (post-market
monitoring)
Article 72 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges the provider of
a high-risk AI system to set up and document a monitoring system
appropriate to the risk and the technology (paragraph 1) that
actively and systematically collects, documents, and analyses
performance data over the entire lifetime of the system
(paragraph 2), is based on a written monitoring plan that is part
of the technical documentation under Annex IV (paragraph 3), and
can be integrated into existing monitoring systems from other EU
harmonisation legal acts (paragraph 4). The monitoring system must
enable the provider to perform a continuous assessment of conformity
with the requirements of Chapter III Section 2 of the EU AI Act;
operationally sensitive data of a law-enforcement authority acting
as a deployer is exempt.
For a forecasting library embedded as a component, the same shared
responsibility described in Chapter
6.1.3
and
Chapter
6.1.5
recurs: the provider of the embedded
high-risk system remains the primary addressee of the monitoring
duty.
spotforecast2-safe
provides the technical
infrastructure from which the required monitoring system can be
fed and is organised into three coordinated data sources.
1.
Operational log instrumentation.
The audit-log schema
v1.0.0 described in Chapter
6.1.5
produces, per
forecasting run, a timestamp-named JSON file under
~/spotforecast2_safe_models/logs/
with
timestamp_utc
at microsecond resolution, an event slug
(
event
), log level, the name of the originating task
(
task
), and a free-form context dictionary. A monitoring
system can collect these files via arbitrary log-aggregation
tools (ELK stack, Grafana Loki, Splunk) and thereby implement the
active and systematic performance capture across the lifetime
required by Art. 72(2) of the EU AI Act, without the package
having to ship its own telemetry infrastructure.
2.
Lifecycle metadata of the deployment instance.
Every
Forecaster
object persists the provenance of the
training data and the model hyperparameters (see
Chapter
6.1.3
); the CPE identifier (see
Chapter
5.6
) identifies the package version
used in every derived SBOM; the Semantic-Release pipeline (see
Chapter
6.1.6
) documents in
CHANGELOG.md
machine-readably how successive versions
differ from one another. Together, these artefacts supply the
monitoring system with the information necessary for the
continuous conformity assessment required by Art. 72(2) of the
EU AI Act, because the provider can, for every stored log entry,
reconstruct the exact combination of package version, training
data, and configuration.
3.
Automated supply-chain monitoring.
The workflows
codeql.yml
,
scorecard.yml
, and Dependabot
presented in Chapter
6.1.8
and
Chapter
6.1.13
continuously monitor code quality,
supply chain, and dependencies. They cover the dimension of
“interaction with other AI systems” contemplated within
Art. 72(2) of the EU AI Act, in so far as that dimension is
mediated through third-party components, and feed their results
into the same advisory pipeline that also serves Art. 73 of the
EU AI Act. For the provider, this means that the monitoring duty
under Art. 72 of the EU AI Act and the reporting duty under
Art. 73 of the EU AI Act rest on the same event chain and need
not be maintained in two separate systems.
The monitoring plan required under Art. 72(3) of the EU AI Act is
part of the technical documentation and finds its written
expression in the
MODEL_CARD.md
(see
Chapter
6.1.4
). The German law implementing the
EU AI Act (KI-Marktüberwachungs- und Innovationsförderungsgesetz,
KI-MIG) is, as of 1 April 2026, in the Bundesrat’s statement
following the federal-government briefing. Adoption is expected by
the end of 2026. Among the planned supplementary information, a
template for Art. 72(3) of the EU AI Act may be issued.
The Commission has not made use of the empowerment under Art. 72(3)
of the EU AI Act to issue a model, and there are deliberations
about deleting this empowering provision in a future revision of
the EU AI Act. The background is the idea of preserving maximum
flexibility for the development and deployment of high-risk AI.
For a provider that simultaneously bears duties under other EU
harmonisation legal acts (e.g. the NIS-2 Directive, or for medical
high-risk products MDR/IVDR), paragraph 4 permits the integration
of the EU AI Act requirements into the existing monitoring system,
or the integration of the QMS from other sectoral legal provisions
into the EU AI Act QMS, in order to avoid double documentation
(Recital 81 of the EU AI Act). The unified log and release trail of
the package supports this integration because it is
machine-readable and can be fed into any existing SIEM or
device-surveillance system.
6.1.13
Article 73 of the EU AI Act (reporting of serious
incidents)
Article 73 of the EU AI Act
[
24
]
obliges the provider of
a high-risk AI system to report every serious incident to the
market surveillance authority of the affected Member State
(paragraph 1). The deadlines are graded: at the latest 15 days
after becoming aware in the standard case (paragraph 2), at the
latest 2 days for widespread infringements or incidents within the
meaning of Art. 3 No. 49(b) of the EU AI Act (paragraph 3), at the
latest 10 days in the case of death (paragraph 4). An initially
incomplete first report may be supplemented by a complete follow-up
report (paragraph 5). After the report, the provider promptly
conducts its own investigations (risk assessment and corrective
measures) and cooperates with the competent authorities;
unilateral changes to the system that could impair a subsequent
root-cause analysis are prohibited (Art. 73(6) of the EU AI Act).
For a forecasting library embedded as a component into a high-risk
AI system, a shared responsibility applies here too: the provider
of the embedded system, regularly the deployer-integrator that
puts the library to use, remains the primary addressee of the
reporting duty. The package provides three mechanisms that enable
the provider to meet the deadlines of Art. 73 of the EU AI Act
technically and to settle the investigation and correction chain
required by Art. 73(6) of the EU AI Act in a verifiable manner.
1.
Confidential reporting channel and version policy.
The
file
SECURITY.md
in the repository establishes GitHub
Private Security Advisories as the primary channel for
confidential reporting; as a fallback, the maintainers’ email
address is available. Public issues are explicitly excluded.
The published response commitments (acknowledgement within
24 hours, initial assessment within 3 working days, coordinated
public disclosure only after a correction has been made
available) are temporally compatible with the 2-day and 10-day
deadlines of Art. 73(3) and (4) of the EU AI Act.
SECURITY.md
additionally documents the window of
supported versions (0.3.x with full support until October 2027,
0.2.x with limited support until February 2026), so that the
provider can clarify, before reporting, on which version the
investigation will be based; the
CODEOWNERS
file names
the maintainers responsible for releases and makes the
cooperation axis required by Art. 73(6) of the EU AI Act
traceable.
2.
Early-warning signals.
Three automated workflows supply
providers and maintainers with early warnings before an incident
reaches market surveillance: the
codeql.yml
workflow for
static security analysis, the
scorecard.yml
workflow
for the weekly OpenSSF Scorecard assessment of the supply chain
(see Chapter
6.1.8
), and Dependabot for
dependency and supply-chain alerts. In IEC 62443-4-1 terminology,
these workflows fill the process area
DM
(Defect
Management); in EU AI Act terminology they create the knowledge
state on the basis of which the provider can date the start of
the reporting deadlines at all.
3.
Verifiable corrective measure.
After advisory triage, the
Semantic-Release pipeline (see Chapter
6.1.6
)
produces a new signed release with the corresponding
correction. Delivery integrity is secured through Sigstore-based
keyless OIDC signatures and entry into the Rekor transparency
log; the accompanying CycloneDX SBOM (see
Chapter
5.6
) allows the recipient to
reconcile against the reported vulnerability. The
CHANGELOG.md
entry references the advisory ID, so that
the verification of an investigation-based correction chain
required by Art. 73(6) of the EU AI Act (commit trail through
release to publication log) is produced without additional
manual effort.
For cases in which the provider or deployer simultaneously bears
duties under other EU legal acts (NIS-2 Directive, MDR/IVDR for
medical high-risk products, CRA Annex II for products with digital
elements) and corresponding reporting duties under the EU AI Act,
Art. 73(9) and (10) of the EU AI Act reduces the reporting matter
to Art. 3 No. 49(c) of the EU AI Act; the mechanisms described
above cover this reduced scope equally, because the advisory,
release, and SBOM trails remain identical. What the library cannot
do is take the substantive decision whether a concrete incident
constitutes a serious incident within the meaning of Art. 3 No. 49
of the EU AI Act; this subsumption remains the provider’s task.
6.2
Relation to the Cyber Resilience
Act
The CRA
[
25
]
, in force since 10 December 2024 and with
its substantive duties applicable from 11 December 2027, imposes on
manufacturers of “products with digital elements” essential
cybersecurity requirements (Annex I) and a 24-month vulnerability-
handling duty (Annex II). A forecasting library shipped as a wheel
on PyPI is a CRA product with digital elements as soon as it is
made available on the Union market in the course of a commercial
activity. The Commission Implementing Decision C(2025) 618 of
3 February 2025 (standardisation request M/606) commissions the
European Committee for Standardisation (CEN), the European
Committee for Electrotechnical Standardisation (CENELEC), and the
European Telecommunications Standards Institute (ETSI) to develop
41 harmonised standards; type-C product-category standards are
foreseen by 30 October 2026, type-B technical-measures standards
by 30 October 2027. EN IEC 62443-4-1 is among the expected type-B
reference standards. An adaptation to 62443-4-1 already in place
today is therefore at the same time the most cost-effective path to
CRA conformity assessment in 2027. NIS-2
[
21
]
already
applies; Art. 21(2)(e) NIS-2 obliges essential and important
entities to take measures for “security in the procurement,
development, and maintenance of network and information systems”
— the SDL evidence from Table
6
satisfies
that part of this duty which a software supplier can satisfy on its
own.
6.3
Legal-Requirements-Engineering
Table
LABEL:tbl-compliance-en
sets out the LRE traceability matrix
for the present package. Each table row names a clause or
requirement together with the mechanism in the package that
addresses it. Figure
5
summarises the
mapping visually.
Tabelle 10:
Mapping of regulatory provisions
to package mechanisms. EU AI Act = Regulation (EU) 2024/1689;
IEC61508 = IEC 61508-3:2010; ISO26262 = ISO 26262-6:2018;
IEC62443 = ISA/IEC 62443 standards series; CRA = Regulation (EU)
2024/2847 (Cyber Resilience Act); NIS-2-RL = Directive (EU)
2022/2555.
Provision
Mechanism
EU AI Act Art. 9 (risk management across the entire lifecycle)
The four process rules of Chapter
4
form the risk-management framework; STRIDE threat model per network-facing module; threat entries are updated in the same pull request as the corresponding code change.
EU AI Act Art. 10 (data governance)
Explicit
on_missing
contract; no silent imputation; provenance of the training data recorded in
MODEL_CARD.md
.
EU AI Act Art. 11 and Annex IV (technical documentation)
MODEL_CARD.md
maintained in the repository for intended purpose, data provenance, metrics, and limits; automatically generated API documentation via quartodoc.
EU AI Act Art. 12 (record-keeping / automatic logs)
Double-handler logger: console plain text, file sink JSON to a fixed schema (
audit_log_schema.json
);
SCHEMA_VERSION
is derived from the schema, schema changes are bound by the CI job
audit-log-schema-gate
to a Conventional-Commits subject
feat!:
and force a major bump via Semantic-Release.
EU AI Act Art. 13 (transparency)
Executable docstrings for every public symbol; fixed public API in
__all__
; CHANGELOG produced by Semantic-Release.
EU AI Act Art. 15 (accuracy, robustness, cybersecurity)
Deterministic transformations; blocklist of forbidden dependencies; CPE identifier for SBOM.
ISO/IEC 25059:2023 (SQuaRE quality model for AI systems — technical counterpart to Art. 15 EU AI Act)
Functional correctness through deterministic transformations; robustness through typed configuration and the fail-safe
on_missing
contract; controllability through the prohibition of silent auto-adaptation documented in Chapter
4
.
EU AI Act Art. 17 (quality-management system — umbrella provision)
QMS documented as: (i) compliance strategy via the process rules of Chapter
4
; (ii) design control in
CONTRIBUTING.md
; (iii) verification and validation in the CI workflows; (iv) data management in the Art. 10 row; (v) risk management in the Art. 9 row; (vi) post-market monitoring and incident reporting in the rows on Art. 72 and Art. 73; (vii) record-keeping in the rows on Art. 12 and Art. 18; (viii) resource and responsibility framework in
MODEL_CARD.md
Section 5 and
CODEOWNERS
.
EU AI Act Art. 18 (provider record-keeping — documentation, logs, conformity evidence)
The repository itself is the records archive: Conventional-Commits history, Semantic-Release tags,
CHANGELOG.md
, release-specific
MODEL_CARD.md
,
uv.lock
snapshot per tag. Records are retained over the ten-year period of Art. 18 EU AI Act via the git history and GitHub releases.
EU AI Act Art. 72 (post-market monitoring)
Structured audit log under
˜/spotforecast2_safe_models/logs/
; CPE identifier enables vulnerability tracking after release.
EU AI Act Art. 73 (reporting of serious incidents to authorities)
GitHub Security Advisories as a confidential disclosure channel; CodeQL and Scorecard workflows as early-warning signals; CHANGELOG entries reference advisory IDs; public disclosure via GitHub releases bundled with the corresponding fix.
IEC 61508-3 Section 7.4.7 (requirements for testing software modules)
One test file per module; for new code a coverage of
≥
\geq
80 % is required; coverage gate in the CI.
IEC 61508-3 Section 7.4.8 (requirements for software integration tests)
Top-level console scripts (
tasks/
) are exercised end-to-end in the CI.
IEC 61508-3 Section 7.9 (software verification)
Executable docstrings; CI blocks the merge if docstring examples fail.
ISO 26262-6 Section 8.4.5 (initialisation of variables)
Fail-safe: uninitialised or NaN inputs raise an exception rather than being defaulted to zero.
ISO 26262-6 Section 9.4.3 (component tests)
One test file per module; pytest configuration in
pyproject.toml
in strict mode.
IEC62443-4-1 SM-4 (third-party components)
Pinned
uv.lock
; blocklist of forbidden dependencies; CPE identifier; dual SPDX headers on ported

code.
IEC62443-4-1 SR-2 (threat-model-driven requirements)
Fail-safe

contract; tight exception handling at the network boundary.

Executable docstrings; per-module unit tests; coverage gate
≥
\geq
80 %; CodeQL.

GitHub Security Advisories; CodeQL and Scorecard workflows; tight, CVE-relevant exception handling.

Semantic-Release
→

signed PyPI wheel and GitHub release; Conventional-Commits gate; CHANGELOG.

Deterministic transformations; pinned CPE; Parquet round-trip contract.

Double-handler logger with file-based audit sink under
˜/spotforecast2_safe_models/logs/

check of the lock files against forbidden dependencies.

; no implicit network calls on import.

7
Example: forecasting electric load
This section walks through the complete
spotforecast2-safe
pipeline on a synthetic load series that reproduces the daily and
weekly patterns of the ENTSO-E France time zone. Synthetic rather
than live data are used so that document generation remains
deterministic and offline-capable; the module
downloader/entsoe.py
and an Open-Meteo adapter
[
80
]
provide the corresponding live-data paths for
production deployment.
The tutorial is deliberately written so that a reader with a legal
background and no experience in statistics or time-series analysis
can follow every step. Each of the ten steps below consists of a
short explanation of what happens and why, together with a code
block that is executed live when the report is rendered. We use
exclusively the interface of the
spotforecast2-safe
library;
the actual estimator (LightGBM) and the cyclical feature encoding
are encapsulated in the wrapper classes, so that a user does not
have to call them directly.
7.1
Step 1: turn on the audit log
The library writes every one of its actions into a structured log.
A subsequent supervisory authority can use those records to
reconstruct which data were processed when. If we turn it on before
we set up the model, it captures the initialisation already. This
practice instantiates the record-keeping obligation of Art. 12 of
the EU AI Act and process rule PR-4.
import
logging
from
spotforecast2_safe.manager.logger
import
setup_logging
logger, _log_path
=
setup_logging(level
=
logging.INFO)
logger.info(
"Tutorial run starting: spotforecast2-safe end-to-end example."
)
2026-04-26 16:31:44,195 - task_safe_n_to_1 - INFO - Tutorial run starting: spotforecast2-safe end-to-end example.
7.2
Step 2: generate data
We simulate an electric load series as the sum of four simple
components: a slight upward trend (e.g. rising power consumption
across the quarter), a daily cycle peaking at midday, a weekday
surcharge (load is higher on weekdays than at weekends), and
normally distributed measurement noise. The series is generated
with a fixed seed (starting value) so that it is bit-identical at
every render (code rule CR-2
Determinism
). The index is a
contiguous UTC hourly sequence — the data format that
spotforecast2-safe
expects.
import
numpy
as
np
import
pandas
as
pd
rng
=
np.random.default_rng(seed
=
2026
)
idx
=
pd.date_range(
"2025-01-01"
,
"2025-03-31 23:00"
, freq
=
"h"
, tz
=
"UTC"
)
hour
=
idx.hour.values.astype(
float
)
dow
=
idx.dayofweek.values.astype(
float
)
trend
=
np.linspace(
0
,
2.0
,
len
(idx))
daily
=
4.0
*
np.sin(
2
*
np.pi
*
hour
/
24
-
np.pi
/
2
)
weekly
=
1.5
*
(dow
<
5
).astype(
float
)
noise
=
rng.normal(
0
,
0.5
, size
=
len
(idx))
y
=
pd.Series(
50.0
+
trend
+
daily
+
weekly
+
noise, index
=
idx, name
=
"load"
)
y.head(
3
)
2025-01-01 00:00:00+00:00    47.103439
2025-01-01 01:00:00+00:00    47.757509
2025-01-01 02:00:00+00:00    47.089588
Freq: h, Name: load, dtype: float64
7.3
Step 3: handle data gaps explicitly
Real sensor data occasionally contain gaps. A safety-critical
library must not silently fill them; it requires the deployer to
take an explicit decision.
LinearlyInterpolateTS
is the
explicit opt-in choice and fills gaps by linear interpolation. For
demonstration we insert a NaN value and show that the gap is
closed after the call. This discipline operationalises Art. 10 of
the EU AI Act (data governance) and CR-3
Fail-safe
.
from
spotforecast2_safe
import
LinearlyInterpolateTS
y_with_gap
=
y.copy()
y_with_gap.iloc[
100
]
=
np.nan
y_clean
=
LinearlyInterpolateTS().fit_transform(y_with_gap)
print
(
f"Missing values before:
{
int
(y_with_gap.isna().
sum
())
}
"
)
print
(
f"Missing values after:
{
int
(y_clean.isna().
sum
())
}
"
)
Missing values before: 1
Missing values after:  0
7.4
Step 4: separate training and evaluation
ranges
For time series, the split into training and evaluation data must
not be random, because future information would otherwise leak into
training. We therefore split chronologically: the first two months
are used to fit the model, March is held out for evaluation. This
split is the simplest variant of the representativeness check
required by Art. 10(2)(f) and (g) of the EU AI Act.
y_train
=
y_clean.loc[
"2025-01-01"
:
"2025-03-01"
]
y_eval
=
y_clean.loc[
"2025-03-02"
:]
print
(
f"Training range:
{
y_train
.
index[
0
]
.
date()
}
to
{
y_train
.
index[
-
1
]
.
date()
}
"
f"(
{
len
(y_train)
}
hours)"
)
print
(
f"Evaluation range:
{
y_eval
.
index[
0
]
.
date()
}
to
{
y_eval
.
index[
-
1
]
.
date()
}
"
f"(
{
len
(y_eval)
}
hours)"
)
Training range:   2025-01-01 to 2025-03-01 (1440 hours)
Evaluation range: 2025-03-02 to 2025-03-31 (720 hours)
7.5
Step 5: build cyclical calendar
features
A load time series depends not only on the immediately preceding
values but also on calendar effects: time of day, day of week,
public holidays. So that the model can recognise that, for example,
“22:00” and “02:00” are close together (rather than the
maximally distant hours 22 and 2), the library encodes such features
cyclically using
radial basis functions
(RBF). We give the
ExogBuilder
the two periodicities time-of-day and
day-of-week and set the country code to Germany so that German
public holidays are added automatically as additional columns. The
feature matrix produced this way is part of the technical
documentation required by Art. 11 of the EU AI Act.
from
spotforecast2_safe
import
Period, ExogBuilder
periods
=
[
Period(name
=
"hour"
,      n_periods
=
6
, column
=
"hour"
,      input_range
=
(
0
,
23
)),
Period(name
=
"dayofweek"
, n_periods
=
4
, column
=
"dayofweek"
, input_range
=
(
0
,
6
)),
]
exog_builder
=
ExogBuilder(periods
=
periods, country_code
=
"DE"
)
exog
=
exog_builder.build(y.index.
min
(), y.index.
max
())
print
(
f"Feature matrix:
{
exog
.
shape[
0
]
}
rows,
{
exog
.
shape[
1
]
}
columns"
)
print
(
f"Columns:
{
list
(exog.columns)
}
"
)
Feature matrix: 2160 rows, 12 columns
Columns: [’hour_0’, ’hour_1’, ’hour_2’, ’hour_3’, ’hour_4’, ’hour_5’,
’dayofweek_0’, ’dayofweek_1’, ’dayofweek_2’, ’dayofweek_3’, ’holidays’,
’is_weekend’]
Figure
6
gives a visual impression of the
data basis: at the top the entire load series with a colour
distinction between training and test ranges, at the bottom three
exemplary cyclical feature columns over an example week, in which
the daily and weekly rhythm of the RBF encoding becomes visible.
Abbildung 6:
Overview of the example data.
Top panel: synthetic load series for Q1 2025 with the training (blue)
and evaluation (orange) ranges defined in Step 4; the dashed
vertical line marks the split point. Bottom panel: three cyclical
feature columns from the
exog
matrix over an example week;
values close to a selected hour or selected day of the week
respectively yield a high activation (value close to 1), more
distant ones a low activation (value close to 0).
7.6
Step 6: instantiate and train the
forecaster
The class
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM
encapsulates a LightGBM
estimator with deterministic hyperparameters and automatic lag
management.
Lags
are the past values that the model uses as
input — with
lags=168
the model sees, for every hour, the
last full week as context, which is decisive for the weekly
seasonality (weekday versus weekend). “Recursive” means that the
forecast for one hour serves in turn as input for the next hour
when forecasting several steps into the future. So that the model
reliably learns the recurring daily and weekly patterns, we replace
the default LightGBM estimator with a richer variant with more
trees, a lower learning rate, and a larger leaf count. With the
call to
fit
, the model performs one training round on the
training data.
from
spotforecast2_safe
import
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM
from
lightgbm
import
LGBMRegressor
model
=
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM(iteration
=
0
, lags
=
168
, periods
=
periods, country_code
=
"DE"
)
model.forecaster.estimator
=
LGBMRegressor(
n_estimators
=
600
,
learning_rate
=
0.05
,
num_leaves
=
63
,
min_child_samples
=
10
,
n_jobs
=-
1
,
verbose
=-
1
,
deterministic
=
True
,
force_col_wise
=
True
,
random_state
=
model.random_state,
)
model.fit(y
=
y_train, exog
=
exog.loc[y_train.index])
print
(
f"Estimator:
{
type
(model.forecaster.estimator)
.
__name__
}
"
)
print
(
f"Number of lags:
{
model
.
forecaster
.
lags
.
size
}
"
)
print
(
f"Number of trees:
{
model
.
forecaster
.
estimator
.
n_estimators
}
"
)
print
(
f"Training index:
{
model
.
forecaster
.
training_range_[
0
]
.
date()
}
"
f"to
{
model
.
forecaster
.
training_range_[
1
]
.
date()
}
"
)

Number of lags:   168
Number of trees:  600
Training index:   2025-01-01 to 2025-03-01
7.7
Step 7: produce a
forecast
We ask the trained forecaster for a day-ahead forecast — the
first 24 hours of the evaluation period. Twenty-four hours is a
common day-ahead forecast horizon in load forecasting, because grid
operators typically have to schedule one day in advance. The model
starts with the last 24 training hours as the initial lag window
and shifts this window recursively, hour by hour.
y_eval_24h
=
y_eval.iloc[:
24
]
y_pred
=
model.forecaster.predict(steps
=
24
, exog
=
exog.loc[y_eval_24h.index])
y_pred.name
=
"forecast"
y_pred.head(
3
)
2025-03-02 00:00:00+00:00    48.091417
2025-03-02 01:00:00+00:00    48.048333
2025-03-02 02:00:00+00:00    47.640752
Freq: h, Name: forecast, dtype: float64
7.8
Step 8: measure accuracy
Two standard measures quantify the deviation between forecast and
the actually observed load. The mean absolute error (MAE) gives the
average deviation in the units of the load series; the mean
squared error (MSE) penalises large deviations
disproportionately. Both are required for the accuracy requirement
of Art. 15 of the EU AI Act.
from
spotforecast2_safe.manager.metrics
import
calculate_metrics
m
=
calculate_metrics(actual
=
y_eval_24h, predicted
=
y_pred)
rmse
=
float
(np.sqrt(m[
"MSE"
]))
mape
=
float
(np.mean(np.
abs
((y_eval_24h.values
-
y_pred.values)
/
y_eval_24h.values)))
print
(
f"MAE  =
{
m[
'MAE'
]
:.3f}
(mean absolute deviation in load units)"
)
print
(
f"MSE  =
{
m[
'MSE'
]
:.3f}
"
)
print
(
f"RMSE =
{
rmse
:.3f}
(square root of MSE, in load units)"
)
print
(
f"MAPE =
{
mape
:.3%}
(mean percentage deviation)"
)
MAE  = 0.494   (mean absolute deviation in load units)
MSE  = 0.346
RMSE = 0.588        (square root of MSE, in load units)
MAPE = 0.960%        (mean percentage deviation)
Figure
7
shows the 24 h forecast together
with the observed load, the model fit to the last 24 training
hours, and a naïve weekly-persistence baseline (load at the same
hour seven days earlier) on a 48-hour window; it follows
structurally the depiction used by Nicolas Chagnet
17
17
17
https://nicolaschagnet.github.io/energy-demand-forecast/
.
Abbildung 7:
Comparison of the 24-hour load
forecast with the observed load and a weekly-persistence baseline.
The window covers the last 24 training hours plus the first 24
evaluation hours; the dashed vertical line marks the end of the
training period. Observed: actual load; In-sample model: model fit
over the closing phase of training; Model forecast: 24 h forecast;
Weekly persistence: load at the same hour seven days earlier as a
trivial baseline. The MAE and MAPE values in the legend refer in
each case to their associated 24-hour window.
7.9
Step 9: rolling-origin
backtest
A single train-test split tests the model’s quality at only one
point of the time axis. A
rolling-origin backtest
shifts
the split point step by step through the time series and runs a
24-hour forecast for every step. From this, an entire distribution
of accuracy measurements arises, robust against individual
outliers and supporting the representativeness check required by
Art. 10(3) of the EU AI Act empirically. The forecasts collected in
this way are also the data basis for the residual analysis in
Figure
8
.
from
spotforecast2_safe.model_selection
import
(
TimeSeriesFold,
backtesting_forecaster,
)
cv
=
TimeSeriesFold(
steps
=
24
,
initial_train_size
=
len
(y_train),
refit
=
False
,
fixed_train_size
=
False
,
verbose
=
False
,
)
metrics_df, predictions_df
=
backtesting_forecaster(
forecaster
=
model.forecaster,
y
=
y_clean,
cv
=
cv,
metric
=
"mean_absolute_error"
,
exog
=
exog,
show_progress
=
False
,
verbose
=
False
,
)
print
(
f"Number of folds:
{
len
(metrics_df)
}
"
)
print
(metrics_df.head())
Number of folds: 1
   mean_absolute_error
0             0.544336
Abbildung 8:
Residuals of the 24-hour forecasts
from the rolling-origin backtest in Step 9. Left: histogram with an
overlaid normal density, fitted to the empirical moments. Right:
sorted residuals against standard-normal quantiles (QQ diagram).
The concentration of points around the diagonal speaks for an
approximately normally distributed error structure.
7.10
Step 10: provenance and
reproducibility
To finish, we inspect the traces left by the pipeline just run.
The trained forecaster knows its estimator, the number of lags
used, the seed, and the index range on which it was fitted. These
items, together with the audit log from Step 1, form the minimum
provenance information that a provider needs for the technical
documentation required by Art. 11 of the EU AI Act and for the
record-keeping obligation of Art. 12 of the EU AI Act.
print
(
f"Model fitted:
{
model
.
forecaster
.
is_fitted
}
"
)
print
(
f"Training index:
{

.

.

0
]
.

}
"
f"to
{
model
.
forecaster
.
training_range_[
1
]
.
date()
}
"
)
print
(

{

(model.forecaster.estimator)

}

)

From these eleven steps the value-add of the library can be
summarised: a user instantiates
one
forecaster class, calls
two
methods (
fit
and
predict
), and obtains,
alongside the forecast, the audit traces that regulatory auditors
can later inspect without further effort. The code and process
rules defined in Chapter
4
are formulated not
as recommendations but are technically anchored in the named
functions.
8
Discussion
This report constitutes a first approximation, with no claim to
completeness, of the development of an EU-AI-Act-compliant package
for time-series point forecasting. The EU has declared the “digital
decade”. The EU AI Act is only one of several planned regulations
and directives. As-yet-unknown future threat scenarios will lead the
legislator to develop further regulations protecting critical
infrastructure (KRITIS), which may affect the development of AI
applications. It is therefore to be expected that the requirements
for the development of AI applications in the KRITIS sector will
continue to grow over the coming years. By means of a consistently
applied PDCA cycle,
spotforecast2-safe
is continuously
developed further in order to meet the evolving regulatory
requirements and to establish the practice of EU-AI-Act-compliant
development in time-series forecasting.
The scope of the package described here is deliberately narrow.
Three classes of functionality (interactive visualisation,
hyperparameter search, deep-learning backends) are explicitly
out of scope and are not included. They are provided by the sister
package
spotforecast2
[
5
]
, which is not
subject to the EU-AI-Act conformance rules. The package
spotforecast2_safe
described here concentrates on
delivering a robust, transparent, and verifiable core for
time-series point forecasting that satisfies the requirements of
the EU AI Act. To this end, LRE principles are embedded into the
library architecture in order to ensure traceability from regulatory
requirements to code mechanisms (compliance-by-design), to maintain
the technical documentation, and to ensure compliance with
record-keeping obligations. A review of the literature and of the
relevant repositories shows (as of April 2026) that the LRE
discipline has not previously been applied in the area of
Python-based time-series forecasting: neither
sktime
[
63
]
,
Darts
[
33
]
,
skforecast
[
2
]
, nor the classical libraries
statsmodels
and
pmdarima
carries bidirectional
traceability between regulatory provisions and code mechanisms,
repository-checked conformity evidence, or contracts built into the
code with respect to the EU AI Act, the CRA, or the ISA/IEC 62443
standards series. In adjacent application domains, however, several
open-source tools exist that operationalise LRE-adjacent practice
without referring to time-series forecasting: COMPL-AI
[
31
]
(ETH Zürich, INSAIT, LatticeFlow AI; Apache 2.0)
provides the first technical interpretation of the EU AI Act and
an open-source benchmarking suite for large language models;
TechOps
[
65
]
provides open templates for the technical
documentation of data, models, and applications across the entire
lifecycle as required by Annex IV of the EU AI
Act
18
18
18
https://aloosley.github.io/techops/
; AIR
Blackbox
[
3
]
and the MCP-EU-AI-Act-Scanner
[
4
]
are CLI tools that check Python codebases
against the requirements of Art. 9 to Art. 11 of the EU AI Act and
generate Annex IV evidence packages; Fides
[
19
]
(Ethyca, Apache 2.0) operationalises a privacy-as-code platform
for the General Data Protection Regulation. What all those tools
have in common is that they act
outside
the library — as
scanners, templates, or a runtime compliance layer.
spotforecast2-safe
takes the inverse approach and brings
LRE to bear
inside
the library, baked into
application-programming-interface contracts, persistence formats,
and continuous-integration gates; in the area of Python time-series
forecasting this is, to our knowledge, conceptually novel.
This report grew out of practical work on an open-source package
that operationalises EU-AI-Act-compliant development and considers
the regulatory requirements from the perspective of a software
developer.
Conflict of Interest Declaration
The authors declare that no conflicts of interest in the sense of
the relevant editorial standards exist. Both authors are
shareholders of Bartz & Bartz GmbH, which publishes the package
spotforecast2-safe
as open source under the Affero General
Public License (AGPL) 3.0-or-later. Beyond this sponsorship there
are no financial or non-financial relationships with third parties
that could have influenced the result of this report.

A
Model card (
MODEL_CARD.md
) of
spotforecast2-safe
This appendix reproduces verbatim the model card published in the
repository under
MODEL_CARD.md
; it follows the taxonomy
of the Hugging Face Model Card Guidebook
[
71
]
and is
the authoritative source for audits. The reproduction corresponds
to the state of the
main
branch at the time of this report;
in the repository itself the file is re-tagged at every release and
is linked to the CPE identifier described in
Chapter
5.6
.
MODEL_CARD.md (verbatim)

71
]
.
A.1
A.1 Model Details
•
Name
: spotforecast2-safe
•
Version
: 1.0.1
•
Type
: Deterministic library for time series transformation
and feature generation (preprocessing + recursive forecasting
wrappers).
•
Developed by
: Thomas Bartz-Beielstein. ORCID:
0000-0002-5938-5158
.
•
Shared by
:
sequential-parameter-optimization
GitHub
organization.
•
Language
: Python 3.13+.
•
License
: AGPL-3.0-or-later.
•
Core dependencies
:
numpy
,
pandas
,
scikit-learn
,
lightgbm
,
numba
,
pyarrow
,
requests
,
feature-engine
,
holidays
,
astral
,
tqdm
(minimal dependency
footprint).
•
Prohibited dependencies
:
plotly
,
matplotlib
,
spotoptim
,
optuna
,
torch
,
tensorflow
.
•
Repository
:
https://github.com/sequential-parameter-optimization/spotforecast2-safe
•
Technical report
:
bart26h/index.qmd
(shipped
in-tree).
•

A.2
A.2 Uses
A.2.1
Direct Use
•
Safety-critical forecasting pipelines
: Preparation of time
series data for regression models in auditable environments (energy
supply, finance, industrial monitoring).
•
Embedded / edge AI
: Runs in resource-constrained environments
where heavyweight ML frameworks are not available.
•
Reproducible research
: Bit-level reproducible N-to-1 lag
transformations, no hidden stochastics.
A.2.2
Downstream Use
•
Feeding the generated feature matrices into
scikit-learn
regressors,
lightgbm
, or
xgboost
via the bundled
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM
/
ForecasterRecursiveXGB
wrappers.
•
Building custom recursive multi-step forecasters on top of the
ForecasterRecursiveModel
base class in
spotforecast2_safe.manager.models
.
A.2.3
Out-of-Scope Use
•
Interactive visualization
: Deliberately no plotting code
ships in this package; no Plotly, no Matplotlib.
•
Automated hyperparameter tuning
: Must run outside the safe
environment (e.g., in a separate
spotforecast2
/
spotoptim
/ Optuna workflow).
•
Silent data cleaning
:
NaN
/
Inf
values
raise
ValueError
; the package does not silently impute.
A.3
A.3 Bias, Risks, and
Limitations
•
Downstream regressor drift
:
spotforecast2-safe
performs deterministic feature engineering, not forecasting by itself.
Accuracy is bounded by the regressor and the training data; concept
drift, seasonality shifts, or regime changes in the downstream model
will silently degrade forecasts.
•
Lag-feature leakage when bypassing
ExogBuilder
:
Users who construct lag or calendar features outside the provided
builders risk leaking target values into the feature row for timestamp
t
. The bundled
ExogBuilder
/
task_n_to_1
paths are leakage-free by construction; hand-rolled pipelines are not.
•
Multi-threaded inference determinism
: The bit-level
reproducibility guarantee assumes single-threaded execution or an
explicitly pinned
n_jobs=1
on the downstream regressor.
Thread-pool schedulers (e.g., LightGBM’s default
n_jobs=-1
)
may reorder floating-point reductions.
•
Memory for large series
: Creating the lag matrix duplicates
the input (
T
×
w
T\times w
). For
T
>
10
7
T>10^{7}
this can exhaust memory;
chunking is the caller’s responsibility.
•
Living standards
: IEC 61508 / ISO 26262 / EU AI Act
references reflect the text as of 2026-04-19. Users must track
subsequent amendments themselves.
A.3.1
Recommendations
•
Validate every new deployment against historical ground truth before
switching traffic.
•
Always use
ExogBuilder
or
task_n_to_1
for feature
construction. Do not hand-roll lag matrices.
•
Pin
n_jobs=1
(or equivalent) on the downstream regressor
when bit-level reproducibility is required.
•
For
T
>
10
7
T>10^{7}
, process the series in windowed chunks and
re-aggregate downstream.
A.4
A.4 How to Get Started
pip
install spotforecast2-safe
from
lightgbm
import
LGBMRegressor
from
spotforecast2_safe
import
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM, ConfigEntsoe
config
=
ConfigEntsoe()
forecaster
=
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM(regressor
=
LGBMRegressor(n_jobs
=
1
), config
=
config)
forecaster.fit(y
=
y_train, exog
=
exog_train)
predictions
=
forecaster.predict(steps
=
config.forecast_horizon, exog
=
exog_future)
A full end-to-end reference workflow (baseline + covariates + LightGBM
vs. ground truth) is registered as a console script:
uv
run spotforecast-safe-demo
The demo source lives in
src/spotforecast2_safe/tasks/task_safe_demo.py
.
A.5
A.5 Technical
Specifications
The core task
task_n_to_1
implements a deterministic
sliding-window transformation.
A.5.1
Mathematical Description
Given a univariate time series
X
=
{
x
1
,
x
2
,
…
,
x
T
}
X=\{x_{1},x_{2},\ldots,x_{T}\}
and a
window size
w
w
(lags), the system produces a feature matrix
X
feat
X_{\mathrm{feat}}
and target vector
y
y
via
X
row
,
t
=
[
x
t
−
w
,
x
t
−
w
+
1
,
…
,
x
t
−
1
]
→
y
t
=
x
t
.
X_{\mathrm{row},t}=[x_{t-w},x_{t-w+1},\ldots,x_{t-1}]\rightarrow y_{t}=x_{t}.
A.5.2
Design Objectives
•
Deterministic
: Same input gives the same bit-level output.
•
Leakage-free
: The target
y
t
y_{t}
is never contained in the
corresponding input vector
X
row
,
t
X_{\mathrm{row},t}
.
•
Fail-safe
: Invalid input raises an explicit exception; the
library never silently repairs bad data.
A.5.3
Architecture (layered)
forecaster/
(low-level estimator wrappers)
→
\rightarrow
preprocessing/
(deterministic transformers:
ExogBuilder
,
RepeatingBasisFunction
,
QuantileBinner
,
TimeSeriesDifferentiator
)
→
\rightarrow
model_selection/
(time-aware CV:
TimeSeriesFold
,
OneStepAheadFold
,
backtesting_forecaster
)
→
\rightarrow
manager/
(orchestration:
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM
,
ForecasterRecursiveXGB
,
ConfigEntsoe
)
→
\rightarrow
processing/
(high-level pipelines)
→
\rightarrow
tasks/
(console-script entry points).
A.6
A.6 Evaluation
No training step runs inside
spotforecast2-safe
itself, so
classical “accuracy” metrics do not apply to the library. The
evaluation targets are software-quality metrics that support compliance
with IEC 61508 / EU AI Act.
A.6.1
Testing Data
•
Docstring examples in
src/
(executed via
tests/test_docstring_examples_*.py
).
•
Unit fixtures and integration data in
tests/
and
src/spotforecast2_safe/datasets/csv/
.
•
The bundled ENTSO-E demo set (
DemoConfig.data_path
) for
end-to-end task tests.
A.6.2
Factors
•
Input dtype (numpy vs. pandas, int vs. float vs. datetime index).
•
Presence of
NaN
/
Inf
(must fail loudly).
•
Series length (smoke-tested up to
10
6
10^{6}
rows).
•
Lag-window size and forecast horizon.
A.6.3
Metrics
•
Functional correctness of the lag-matrix transformation (unit tests).
•
CPE identifier generation (
tests/test_cpe.py
).
•
Determinism: identical input must yield identical output bytes.
•
Coverage: at least 80 % line coverage on new code (see
CONTRIBUTING.md
).
A.6.4
Results
•
Fail-safe behavior
: DataFrames containing
NaN
or
Inf
raise
ValueError
. Public loaders
(
load_timeseries
,
load_timeseries_forecast
,
WeatherService.get_dataframe
) refuse to return
silently-imputed values by default. Callers must opt in to legacy
forward/back-fill via
on_missing='ffill_bfill'
(loaders) or
fill_missing=True
(weather client) to restore
pre-1.0 behavior.
•
Input validation
: Strict type hinting plus runtime checks for
pd.DataFrame
and
np.ndarray
.
•
Cybersecurity footprint
: The prohibited-dependency policy
minimizes the Common Vulnerabilities and Exposures (CVE) attack
surface. No web server, no deep-learning runtime, no plotting backend.
A.7
A.7 Environmental Impact
The library itself performs no training and requires no GPU. Runtime
cost is dominated by (a) NumPy/Pandas vector ops during feature
engineering and (b) whatever downstream regressor the caller passes in.
A typical
ForecasterRecursiveLGBM
fit on a
10
5
10^{5}
-row series
with 168 lags completes in seconds on a single commodity CPU core; the
per-inference carbon cost of the safe layer is effectively negligible
next to the regressor’s own cost. No pretrained weights are shipped, so
there are no embedded-training emissions to report.
A.8
A.8 Compliance & EU AI Act
Support
This package is designed to support the development of high-risk AI
systems according to the EU AI Act.
The package itself is not
certified
; the system integrator owns full-system certification.
•
Art. 10 (Data Governance)
: The package rejects dirty data
(
NaN
/
Inf
) by default, supporting governance
requirements for training and inference data quality.
•
Art. 11 (Technical Documentation)
: This card plus the
bart26h/
technical report form the technical-documentation
baseline. The CPE identifier in §A.1 feeds SBOM and
vulnerability-tracking pipelines.
•
Art. 12 (Automatic Logging)
:
spotforecast2_safe.manager.logger
provides a dual-handler
(console + persistent file) logger; tasks emit timestamped logs under
~/spotforecast2_safe_models/logs/
for audit
retention.
•
Art. 13 (Transparency)
: Code is “white-box”; no compiled
inference kernels, no opaque model weights.
•
Art. 15 (Accuracy & Robustness)
: Transformations are
mathematically provable and bit-level reproducible. Formal
verification remains the user’s responsibility.
See the compliance table in the accompanying technical report
(
bart26h/index.qmd
, section
Compliance Mapping
) for the
authoritative mapping to IEC 61508, ISO 26262, ISA/IEC 62443, and the EU
AI Act articles.
